# A1 William Morris, The Story of the Glittering Plain THE STORY OF THE GLITTERING / PLAIN. WHICH HAS BEEN ALSO / CALLED THE LAND OF LIVING / MEN OR THE ACRE OF THE UN-/DYING. WRITTEN BY WILLIAM / MORRIS. COLOPHON: HERE endeth the Glittering Plain, printed by/ William Morris at the Kelmscott Press, Up-/per Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of / Middlesex: and finished on the 4th day of/ April of the year 1891. / [paragraph] Sold by Reeves & Turner 196 Strand London. COLLATION: Small 4°: a² b-2a4 2b². 96 leaves, Pp. [4] [1] 2-188. PAPER & DIMENSIONs: Flower(I) paper. Leaf: 199 × 140 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTS: Border I; six- and ten-line initials. CONTENT\$: ar-a1ν blank a2 title a2 table of contents b-2b2 text 2b2 colophon. BINDING: Stiff vellum with washleather ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘THE /STORY /OF/THE /GLIT-/TER-/ING/PLAIN BY / WILL-/IAM / MORRIS / 1891.'[Golden type]. PUBLICATION: 200 paper copies (2 gns.); 6 vellum copies (12 and 15 gns.). Issued 8 May 1891. NOTEs: The Golden Legend (A7) was originally intended to be the initial publication of the KP. However, when Batchelor supplied the first delivery of paper to the Press, Morris reported to Ellis that it was‘not sized quite hard enough. . . . But it is only a little lot (9 reams), therefore I intend printing a little edition of the Glittering Plain on it' (Mackail, ii, 268). Morris approached Walter Crane about illustrating it, but,’as Crane late recalled, ‘he was so eager to get his first book out that he could not wait for the pictures, and so the Glittering Plain first appeared simply with his own initials and ornaments'(Crane, An Artist's Reminiscences [1907], p. 328). Hence in 1894 Morris produced another edition of the book (see A22) – the only KP title to be printed twice – with illustrations by Crane. The Glittering Plain was set, with minor alterations, from the English Illustrated Magazine (June-September 189o), where it had been first printed, and the proofs (see j below) show that it was read for the press with unusual care by Morris. He had finished the border, which was engraved by Hooper, by 22 January 189ı (Cockerell diary), and on 31 January the earliest trial pages were pulled, even though the Golden type fount was not yet complete. The first sheet was printed on 2 March (see j below), and work progressed during the following month despite Morris's‘gouty illness’. Cockerell happened to visit the Press for the first time on 4 April, the day the colophon was being printed: Emery Walker took me to see the Kelmscott Press, in which the Glittering Plain, the first book to be issued from it, is now being printed from Morris' new type. Saw the colophon being set up, with the date April 4th 1891. Exceedingly interesting. The house a little old fashioned one the single hand press at the top of a winding corner stair, the compositor being in a little room leading out of that in which the press is. Printed sheets, one on vellum, lying about – all most beautiful, especially the first page with its elaborately designed border’ (Cockerell diary). Cobden-Sanderson noted in his journal on the same day: . . . I have just received from Morris the “dummy"; it is a dummy binding by Leighton, to show how he proposes to bind The Glittering Plain' (The Journals of Thomas James Cobden-Sanderson [New York, 1926], i, 3o4). The volume was the only kP book with washleather ties. ‘Morris had four of the vellum copies bound in green vellum, three of which he gave to friends'(List'; see y below). Wilfrid Scawen Blunt had lunch with the Morrises on 7 May 1891, the day the first copy of the book was delivered: ‘He [Morris] is full as ever of his work and took me to see his printing press a few doors off, and sold me across the dining table the first copy of the first volume printed at the Kelmscott Press, a very wonderful production. . . . While we were sitting talking this copy was brought in to him by the printer, the first that had been bound and made up, and I asked him what do you mean to do with these books?" He said “to sell them of course[.]"“Will you sell this copy to me?"“Certainly[.]"“How much?"“A pound". So I paid my pound and took it away, having made him write his name thus: “I sold this book to Wilfrid Scawen Blunt May 7th 1891. William Morris"'(Blunt diary). An announcement of The Glittering Plain appeared in the Literary Gossip' column of the Athenaeum on 4 April 1891 (p. 442), and as a result all copies were disposed of almost immediately (Walker to T. B. Reed, 19 November 1891 [sB]). Of the 200 paper copies, 18o were sold (Morris's first plan, in fact, had been to print only twenty copies for distribution to his friends [Sparling, p. 75]); only two vellum copies were sold. The book was reviewed in the Library, 3 (1891), 233–4. RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Three leaves of the English Illustrated Magazine text, marked by Morris for the KP printers (BL C.102.h.18). (b) Trial pages (Perry collection, nos. 2-3). (c) These were the first [two] trial pages printed at the Kelmscott Press on Jan 31 1891 before the fount was complete. A lower case g is used in them which Morris immediately discarded'(Cockerell's note) (BL C.43.c.26 [ΣQQ 68]). (d) Early trial proof of the first sheet of The Glittering Plain'’(Cockerell's note) (BL C.102.h.18). (e) Trial page (BL C.102.h.18). (f) Trial pages (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], no. 14o). (g) Trial pages (Saks sale, no. 129). (h) Corrected (by Morris) proofs (Dc). (i) ‘A proof of the first page of the first book printed at the Kelmscott Press' (Robert Steele's note) (BL C.43.g.19). (j) Corrected (by Morris) proofs (Cockerell sale, no. 61; now PML ‘Morrisiana 32’). (k) Two proofs of p. I, one inked with a composition roller, the other with a lithographic roller (Jc). (l) Gathering b⁴ with Cockerell's note: ‘The first sheet printed at the Kelmscott Press' (BL C.99.k.29). (m) Pres. copy to Cockerell, 1o May 1891, with designs for two initials, trial pages, and variant of gathering 2b² (Cockerell sale, no. I; now BL). (n) F. S. Ellis's copy with some notes in his hand (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], no. 139). (o) Pres. copy (vellum) to Ellis, 13 June 1891 (pCL). (p) Copy printed on experimental paper, afterwards rejected as too hard, with inscription: ‘To Robert Steele, this “retree" copy, from the corrector for the Press,"Halliday Sparling'(Hodgson cat., 24 April 1929, no. 46). (q) Pres. copy to Crane, 1o May 1891 (Colin Franklin cat. 2 [1971]). (r) Copy with five letters from Morris to Crane (Sotheby cat., 26 July 1919, no. 172a). (s) Pres. copy to Cormell Price, 3 January 1893 (mc). (t) Pres. copy to Isabella Gilmore (wMG). (u) Pres. copy to Kate Faulkner, 30 May 1891 (Swann Galleries cat., 21 October 1976, no. 163). (v) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 8 May 1891 (PML). (w) Pres. copy with letter to Theodore Watts (BL Ashley 3692). (x) Pres. copy to Philip Webb, 21 May 1891 (τc). (y) Pres. copy (vellum) to Philip Webb, 21 May 1891 (RC). (z) Pres. copy to Jenny Morris, 8 June 1891 (FM[1]). (aa) T. B. Reed's copy, with letters to Reed from Morris, II September 1891, and from Emery Walker, 19 May 1891, and with a note by Reed about the casting of the Golden type (sB). (bb) Pres. copy to May Sparling, 16 May 1891 (κm). (cc) Pres. copy (vellum) to Jane Morris (κm). (dd) Morris's copy (vellum), with note by Cockerell: Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (Lilly Library, Indiana University). (ee) T. J. Cobden-Sanderson's copy (BC). (ff) Pres. copy to Oswald Birchall (Sotheby cat., 15 February 1971, no. 214). (gg) Pres. copy to W. H. Hooper, 16 May 1891 (Parke-Bernet cat., 15 May 1941, no. 531). (hh) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 8 May 1891 (Sotheby cat., 22 December 1952, no. 41). (ii) Pres. copy to Phyllis Ellis, 21 October 1891 (American Art Association cat., 1 March 1933, no. 445). ## A2 William Morris, Poems by the Way POEMS BY THE WAY. WRITTEN BY WILLIAM MORRIS. CoLoPHoN: HERE endeth Poems by the Way, written / by William Morris, and printed by him at the / Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, / in the County of Middlesex; and finished on / the 24th day of September of the year 1891. / [paragraph] Sold By Reeves & Turner, 196, Strand, London. / [printer's mark 1] COLLATION: Small 4°: [a]² b⁸(±) c8–n⁸ 04. 102 leaves, PP. [4] [1] 2-197 [198] [2] [=200]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(1) paper. Leaf: 205×I43 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTs: Border 1; six- and ten-line initials; shoulder titles and some lines in red. CoNTENTs: [a]ɪ title [a]ı blank [a]2r-[a]2 table of contents [a]2ν printer's mark 1 bır–o2 text o3 colophon o3–4 blank. BINDING: Stiff vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘POEMS/BY/THE/WAY/BY/WILL-/IAM/MORRIS/i891.’ [Golden type]. PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (2 gns.); 13 vellum copies (about 12 gns.). Issued 20 October 1891. NOTEs: In June 1891 Morris wrote to Reeves & Turner: ‘The title of my new book will be Poems by the Way; the format the same as the Glittering Plain. It will be printed in red and black. The poems will include some recently written and some written many years ago. Some have appeared in magazines, but with two exceptions of a little piece out of the Jason and one out of the Ogier, they will none of them have been printed in any book of mine’ (Mackail, ii, 273). The poems were assembled with the assistance of Charles Fairfax Murray, who had preserved a number of unpublished poems of early date . . . and who also had kept a record of those that had appeared separately in periodicals from time to time' (Introductions, i, 27o). One of the poems, ‘Goldilocks and Goldilocks', was written on 2o May 1891 in order to increase the length of the book. According to Cockerell, Morris first thought of entitling the volume Flores Atramenti (List’). Printing began in July. When Wilfrid Scawen Blunt dined at Kelmscott House on 6 July, he noticed ‘some sheets of his new volume of poems which is to be printed uniform with the volume he is printing for me. Some of the lines are in red, which has a capital effect' (Blunt diary). On 14 July, from Folkestone, Morris wrote to Walker: ‘Yes, I think it would be best to print that sheet again; a misprint of that kind, though it does not otherwise matter, is such defacement of the book, – and 'tis a 2 guinea book let us remember.' Walker later annotated this letter by adding that the misprint was, he thought, in Poems by the Way, and that he had ordered it reprinted while Morris was absent (HRC). The misprint was probably‘those'for thou'on p. 19, 1. 13 (see j below). We catch another glimpse of the book in production in Blunt's diary on 7 August 1891: Lunched at Kelmscott House where Mrs. Morris took me to see the printing. Morris's own poems were being struck off, most beautiful they are with their rubrics. The sheet I saw being printed contained the Ballad of John a Wood.' A rejected variant of the last gathering (see n below) shows that Morris rearranged the colophon at the last moment in order to obtain more even word-spacing. He was apparently so pleased with his new printer's mark that he used it twice: at the end of the table of contents and on the colophon page. ‘They are printing the colophon sheet today, which is exciting,’Morris wrote his daughter Jenny on 23 September 1891 (Mackail, ii, 279). Cockerell, calling at Kelmscott House on 5 November, observed ‘several copies of Poems by the Way on the dining room table with covers stained red, yellow, blue & green' (Cockerell diary). Roberts Brothers of Boston, Mass., issued an unauthorised photographic facsimile of Poems by the Way in 1891. An authorised inexpensive edition was published by Reeves & Turner in December of that year. RELATED MATERIALS: (a) MS of text (HEH HM 6427). (b) Trial page, based on a proof of The Story of the Glittering Plain (v & A[2] 1.129). (c) Trial pages (private collection of W. S. Peterson). (d) Trial pages (Manchester Central Library). (e) Inc. proofs (Sotheby cat., 27 March 1945, no. 348). ( f) Inc. proofs (Sotheby cat., 11 May 196o, no. 589). (g) Corrected (by Morris) proofs (Saks sale, nos. 13o-1). (h) Three proofs initialled by Morris (Sotheby cat., 18 December 1944, no. 191). (i) Inc. proofs (PML ‘Morrisiana 11'). (j) Cancelled leaves b3-b6 (pp. 5-12) (c). (k) Corrected (by Morris) proofs (Cockerell sale, no. 62). (l) Gathering b8, with one initial hand-coloured in blue (v & A[2] 1.129). (m) Pres. copy (vellum) to Philip Webb, 18 November, with another inscription from Webb to Cockerell; with design for printer's mark I (Cockerell sale, no. 2). (n) Pres. copy to Cockerell, 1o May 1891, with designs for initials, trial pages and variant of final gathering (BL C.- 102.a.19). (o) Cockerell's copy, with designs for three initials and trial pages (Cockerell sale, no. 3; now [without trial pages] private collection of Virginia Surtees, London). (p) Copy (vellum) with Cockerell's bookplate (LC copy 2 [Rc]). (q) Pres. copy to Charles Fairfax Murray, 21 November 1891 (Sotheby cat., 7 July 1919, no. 122). (r) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 22 October 1891 (Sotheby cat., 7 July 1919, no. 187). (s) Pres. copy to Mrs. Gilmore: ‘Best love from your affectionate Brother William Morris'(wmG). (t) Pres. copy (vellum) to Ellis, 17 December 1891 (Anderson Galleries cat., 24 April 193o, no. 139). (u) Pres. copy to Theodore Watts, 1891 (Sotheby cat., 14 March 1917, no. 486). (v) Pres. copy to Eiríkr Magnússon (Sotheby cat., 6 November 1918, no. 375). (w) Pres. copy to Herbert M. Ellis, 1o June 1892 (Sotheby cat. 23 March 1927, no. 744). (x) Copy (vellum) owned by Morris and bound in red-stained vellum, with a note by Cockerell that it was sold to Henry Gamman in 1897 (Mc). (γ) Pres. copy to Philip Webb, 22 October 1891 (τc). (z) Pres. copy to Jenny Morris, November 189ı (FM-[1]). (aa) Pres. copy to May Morris, 21 October 1891 (κm). (bb) Pres. copy to Jane Morris (κm). (cc) Pres. copy to Phyllis Marion Ellis, 21 October 1891 (Dc). (dd) Pres. copy to W. H. Hooper (Lilly Library, Indiana University). (ee) ‘This book is from the private stock of Mr. William Morris and was sold to me . . . 1896. Jas. Gaunt’(Claremont). (ff) T. J. Cobden-Sanderson's copy (Bc). (gg) Pres. copy (vellum) to Robert H. Benson (Sotheby cat., 7 October 1964, no. 68o). (hh) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 22 October 1891 (Sotheby cat., 22 December 1952, no. 42). (ii) Pres. copy to Kate Faulkner (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], no. 141). A3 Wilfrid Scawen Blunt, The Love-Lyrics and Songs of Proteus THE LOVE-LYRICS & SONGS OF / PROTEUS BY WIL-FRID SCAWEN / BLUNT WITH THE LOVE-SON-/NETS OF PROTEUS BY THE SAME / AUTHOR NOW RE-PRINTED IN / THEIR FULL TEXT WITH MANY / SON-NETS OMITTED FROM THE / EARLIER EDITIONS. / [in red:] LONDON MDCCCXCII. CoLopHoN: HERE end the Love-Lyrics and Songs of Pro-/teus, Written by Wilfrid Scawen Blunt; with / the Love-Sonnets of Proteus by the same Au-/thor. Printed by William Morris at the Kelm-/scott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the / County of Middlesex, and finished on the 26th / day of January of the year 1892. / [paragraph] Sold by Reeves & Turner, 196 Strand, London. / [printer's mark 1] COLLATION: Small 4°: π² [a]⁴ b-q⁸ r6. 132 leaves, Pp. [4] i-vii [viii] [=12] [1] 2-251 [252]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(1) paper. Leaf: 205×142 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTs: Border I; six- and ten-line initials; shoulder titles, initials, stanza and sonnet numbers, and some lines in red. CoNTENTs: π-πIν blank π2 title π2 blank [a]I-[a]4 table of contents bī-r5 text r6 colophon r6 blank. BINDING: Stiff vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘THE /LOVE /LYRICS /AND /SONGS/OF/PROTEUS /BY/ W. S. /BLUNT / 1892'[Golden type]. PUBLICATION: 3oo paper copies (2 gns.); no vellum copies. Issued 27 February 1892. NOTEs: I have put off writing to have more news first of my typefounding adventure,’Morris wrote to Wilfrid Scawen Blunt on 19 November 189o. ‘I think now that I shall be ready to set to work in about 3 months perhaps earlier. I shall of course be very pleased to print your book, if I may have my own way about the get up – which, I take it is what you want.'(This and other letters from Morris to Blunt quoted below are in v & A[1] Box 111.86.kk.) In fact, Blunt did dictate one element of the design; the decorated initials were printed in red at his request, and when the book was in progress Morris commented: ‘The are now on Mr. Blunt's book[.] I have one sheet and it looks very gay & pretty with its red letters, but I think I prefer mine in style of printing’ (Morris to Jenny Morris, 22 October 1891 [BL Add. MS. 45340, f. 73]). Morris reported to Blunt on 23 February 189ı that his type had now arrived and he could begin printing Blunt's poems. The volume was planned mainly as a reprint of Blunt's Sonnets and Songs by Proteus (1875) and The Love Sonnets of Proteus (188o). (In a preface to the latter, Blunt had thus explained his pseudonym: ‘The author of these sonnets, styling himself Proteus, acknowledges thereby a natural mood of change.'A copy of the book, with dates of composition supplied by Cockerell at the bottom of each page, is in the Department of Manuscripts and Printed Books, the Fitzwilliam Museum.) However, he also intended to include a new poem, Natalia's Resurrection', which he decided to place first in the KP volume at Mrs. Morris's urging. Typesetting began at the end of February, though it progressed very slowly because both The Golden Legend (A7) and Poems by the Way (A2) were in the press. The first proofs were not available to Blunt until the following August. But a major problem arose during the next month. On 3o September Blunt recorded in his diary: I have been away for two nights to Kelmscott [Manor] to see Morris about the printing and to wish Mrs. Morris good bye for the winter. . . . We have arranged that Natalia is to be suppressed and instead of it that the old Proteus Sonnets shall be reprinted as the second part of the Kelmscott volume with certain new sonnets and some of those omitted from the earlier editions. The suppression of Natalia was decided on in consequence of a wild letter from Margaret who imagined that the poem described the relations between herself and her husband and me. And, although the poem had really nothing to do with her, she was so urgent that I could not refuse. It put Morris to a great inconvenience, for Natalia was already in print, and I was obliged to explain the circumstances to him, when he at once, and most good naturedly consented to the change.'The circumstances were that Blunt, who was notorious for his sexual escapades, had just concluded an affair with Margaret Talbot, wife of the Military Secretary at the British Embassy in Paris (Elizabeth Longford, A Pilgrim of Passion: The Life of Wilfrid Scawen Blunt [198o], pp. 281–4). The poem does indeed seem to allude to the episode. The change involved cancelling eighteen pages of type that had already been set (see a below). Morris's cheerful acquiescence is curious in view of the fact that his wife had also had a prolonged affair with Blunt. (For an account of the relationship, see Peter Faulkner, Wilfrid Scawen Blunt and the Morrises [1981].) Throughout the printing of the book, Janey Morris sent detailed criticisms of the poems to Blunt and proofed the entire volume. (She is not known to have proofread any other κP book.) Lady Gregory also corrected the proofs (Edith Finch, Wilfrid Scawen Blunt, 1840–1922 [1938], p. 287). On 12 March 1892 Morris sent to Blunt a summing-up of the accounts: ‘The 22o copies offered for sale have all been sold, and Reeves has handed me £2oo on account for you. ... I am charging you £i5o for the printing which was a good deal more than I thought it would cost: but the book finally was much longer than I had estimated and the red printing, there being a great deal of it nearly doubled the cost of the presswork. . . . The binding is extra and will cost something over £3o. . . . I am glad that you will make something out of the book; for when Reeves pays up the whole there will be à good balance in your favour, without reckoning the copies you keep. So I think we may congratulate ourselves on a success. As for its critical reception, Morris reported (in a letter to Blunt, 30 April 1892) that the only unfavourable review was by Joseph Pennell in the Star, a ‘cur by profession'. Ellis, however, took a dim view of Blunt's poetical pretensions: ‘The fact is Morris is so good natured that he does not like to say “no" but is printing this book surely against the grain' (Ellis to Francis Jenkinson, 14 March 1892 [CUL Add. 6463, no. 2018]). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Inc. corrected (by Blunt) proofs (Cockerell sale, no. 63; now PML ‘Morrisiana 1'). (b) Inc. corrected proofs (BL C.43.c.26 [∆.d.3]). (c) First proof of title-page (BL C.43.c.26 [∆.d.3]). (d) First proof of title page (BL C.43.c.26 [ΣFF2o]). (e) Pres. copy from Blunt to Cockerell, 12 June 1898 (Cockerell sale, no. 4; Blackwell cat. 655, no. 381). (f) Pres. copy to Cockerell, 2o May 1896 (signed also by Blunt), with notes by Cockerell on dates of composition, etc. (Cockerell sale, no. 5; Sotheby cat., 31 March 1965, no. 607). (g) Pres. copy from Blunt to Caroline Grosvenor, 2 July 1892 (MC). (h) Copy signed by Blunt, 15 August 1899 (Mitchell Library, Glasgow). (o) Pres. copy from Blunt to Gwendolen Irene Bourke (uBC). (p) Pres. copy from Burne-Jones to E.L., Christmas 1892 (Sotheby cat., 10 February 1959, no. 387). (q) Pres. copy from Blunt to Lady Violet Granby (Sotheby cat., 21 November 1966, no. 15s). (r) Pres. copy from Blunt to Dorothy Grosvenor, 29 April 1892 (University of London Library). (s) Pres. copy from Blunt to Frederick Locker, 22 April 1892 (American Art Association cat., I March 1933, no. 431). ## A4 John Ruskin, The Nature of Gothic ## THE NATURE OF GOTHIC A CHAP-/TER OF THE STONES OF VENICE./ BY JOHN RUSKIN. COLoPHoN: HERE ends the Nature of Gothic, by John Rus-/kin, printed by William Morris at the Kelmscott / Press, Hammersmith, and published by George / Allen, 8, Bell Yard, Temple Bar, London, and / Sunnyside, Orpington. / [printer's mark 1] COLLATION: Small 4°: [a]4 b-8. 68 leaves, pP. [3] i-iv [v] [=8] [1] 2- 127 [128]. PAPER & DIMENSIONs: Flower(I) paper. Leaf: 200 × 14I mm. Text: 30-31 lines × 92 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATIONS: Border I; six- and ten-line initials; line-block diagrams scattered throughout text. coNTENTs: [a]1–[a]1ν blank [a]2 title [a]2v-[a]4 preface (by Morris, dated 15 February 1892) [a]4 printer's mark 1 bī–i8v text i8v colophon. BINDING: Stif vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘THE /NATURE /OF/GOTHIC./BY/JOHN/RUSKIN./1892’ [reduced Golden type]. PUBLICATION: 500 paper copies (3os.); no vellum copies. Issued 22 March 1892. NOTEs: The genesis of this volume can best be traced through a series of extracts from the diary of Sydney Cockerell (who was an acquaintance and ardent admirer of Ruskin). 11 June 1891: Home as far as Hammersmith with Morris & Walker. Suggested that Morris should print decent editions of Omar Khayyam and of“On the Nature of Gothic"from the Stones of Venice. Morris seemed to think that he would do so, as The Golden Legend is such a big book that he will have to do some little ones after it is finished.'1 September 1891: Called on George Allen at Bell Yard. . . . Spoke of “The Nature of Gothic" and begged him to get Morris to print it with his new type.' 1o September 1891: Morris ready ready to print the Nature of Gothic if Allen will give him a free hand.'11 September 1891: ‘Called on Mr G. Allen at Bell Yard, and told him that Morris was ready to begin printing the Nature of Gothic very shortly. Mr A. apparently very pleased.'8 October 1891: ‘Copy for the Nature of Gothic received from Allen, though it will not be ready by Christmas.'2ı February 1892: ‘Saw Morris’Introduction to the “Nature of Gothic"in the manuscript – which Sparling offered to give me and I, under sore temptation, refused. It is admirable.'In fact, Cockerell later secured the Ms of Morris's preface from Sparling in exchange for two copies of the book (Cockerell to May Morris, 17 April 1936 [BL Add. Ms. 52740, f. 181]). This chapter of The Stones of Venice (1853) had previously been reprinted as à 4d. pamphlet by F. J. Furnivall on the occasion of the opening of the Working Men's College in 1853. Cook and Wedderburn, in their Library Edition of Ruskin's Works, add the following bibliographical note: ‘The Kelmscott edition was set up from the 1886 edition of the complete work, and some misprints which crept into that issue are repeated. . .. Two misprints peculiar to the Kelmscott may also be noted. On p. 26, lines I1 and 12, the last letters are wrongly spaced; and on p. 56, last line, “God" is printed with a small “g"' (x, lxx). In 1899 George Allen published another separate edition of the chapter which reprinted Morris's preface. The compositors of the KP edition were Collins and Howes (see a below). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) MS and corrected (by Morris) proofs of Morris's preface, earlier printed text of The Nature of Gothic marked up by Morris for the KP printers, proofs of pp. 113, 114, 127, and 128, and Ms in Morris's hand of colophon (FM[1]). (b) Inc. corrected (by Morris) proofs (PML Morrisiana 11’). (c) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 4 April 1892 (Sotheby cat., 7 July 1919, no. 188). (d) Robert Proctor's copy; presented by Cockerell to Thomas Hardy, 191ı, and returned by Hardy's widow, 1928 (Cockerell sale, no. 6; Blackwell cat. 655, no. 385). (e) Pres. copy to May Morris, 2 April 1892 (κm). (f) Pres. copy to Philip Webb, 21 April 1892 (τC). (g) Pres. copy to Ruskin, 11 April 1892 (Sotheby cat., 24 July 193o, no. 91). (h) Pres. copy to Jenny Morris, 2 April 1892 (FM[1]). (i) Walter Crane's copy, with a letter from Cockerell to Crane, 24 September 1892 (íL). (j) Pres. copy to Kate Faulkner, 22 April 1892 (HL). (k) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 3 April 1892 (Sotheby cat., 22 December 1952, no. 44). ## A5 William Morris, The Defence of Guenevere, and Other Poems THE DEFENCE OF GUENEVERE,/ AND OTHER POEMS. BY WILLIAM / MORRIS. COLOPHoN: HERE ends The Defence of Guenevere, and / other Poems, written by William Morris; and/ printed by him at the Kelmscott Press, 14, / Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of/ Middlesex; & finished on the 2nd day of April, / of the year 1892. / [paragraph] Sold by Reeves & Turner, 196, Strand, London. / [printer's mark 1] COLLAT1ON: Small 4°: [a]² b-18 m6. 88 leaves, Pp. [4] [1] 2-169 [170] [2] [=172]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(I) paper. Leaf: 200 × 140 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm. TYPE: Golden type. I am following the K.P. but strange to say though dear old Morris corrected that much as to punctuation – he has not only left the old misprints of 1858 but perpetuated others “sleepby" for “sleepy" &c &c. Sheet d of edition had to be reprinted because Morris's printers mistakenly put Galahad's name in red on p. 34 (see b below). ‘The first sheet was printed on February 17, 1892' (List’). By 21 April Morris had a bound copy in hand (Cockerell diary). During April and early May the title on the spine was being calligraphically inscribed on each copy by F. S. Ellis's son Herbert, a partner in Withall & Ellis, Architects and Surveyors. This was the only kP book with a hand-lettered title, and the first to be bound in limp vellum. The compositors whose names or initials appear on the copytext (see a below) are J. Tippet, T. Binning, and J.P.'(not identified). In no case should the book be pressed, / as that would destroy the “impression" of the type and thus / injure the appearance of the printing. / W. MORRIS. CONTENTS: $\left[ V o l . i \right]$ aı title $\mathsf { a r } ^ { \boldsymbol { \mathsf { V } } }$ blank $\mathsf { a } 2 ^ { \mathtt { r } } - \mathsf { a } 3 ^ { \mathtt { r } }$ introduction (by Caxton) $\mathsf { a } 3 ^ { \mathtt { r } } \mathsf { - a } 4 ^ { \mathtt { v } }$ table of contents $\mathsf { a } \mathsf { S } ^ { \mathsf { r } } \mathsf { - a } 6 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ index $\chi ^ { \mathrm { { I } ^ { I } } }$ blank $\chi \mathrm { I } ^ { \mathrm { V } }$ engraved title ${ \tt b r } _ { - { \tt h } 4 } ^ { \tt r }$ text h $4 ^ { \mathrm { v } _ { - 2 } } x 2 ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ blank $2 \chi 2 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ illustration $\mathrm { h } \varsigma ^ { \mathrm { r } } { - } \mathrm { r } 2 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ text $3 { \chi ^ { \mathrm { I } } } ^ { \mathrm { I } }$ blank $3 { \chi ^ { \mathrm { v } } }$ illustration $\mathrm { r } 3 ^ { \mathrm { r } } { - } 2 \mathrm { g } 8 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ text. [Vol. ii] $\pi 2$ pastedown $2 \pi 3 ^ { \mathfrak { r } } { - } 2 \pi 4 ^ { \mathfrak { v } }$ title $3 \mathrm { k I } ^ { \mathrm { r } } { - } 4 \mathrm { m } 6 ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ text $4 ^ { \mathrm { m } 6 ^ { \mathrm { r } } }$ Caxton's colophon $4 \mathrm { m } 6 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ blank $4 ^ { \mathrm { n } \mathrm { { I } ^ { r } - 4 ^ { n } 3 ^ { r } } }$ glossary $4 ^ { \mathrm { n } 3 ^ { \mathrm { v } } - } 4 ^ { \mathrm { n } \varsigma ^ { \mathrm { v } } }$ ‘MEMORANDA, BIBLIOGRAPHICAL & EX-PLANA-/TORY, CONCERNING THE LEGENDA AUREA OF / JACOBUS DE VORAGINE & SOME OF THE TRANS-/LATIONS OF IT.’ (by Ellis) $4 ^ { \mathrm { n } \varsigma ^ { \mathrm { v } } }$ colophon $4 ^ { \mathrm { n 6 ^ { r } - } } 4 ^ { \mathrm { n 6 ^ { v } } }$ blank. BINDıNG: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). Printed in black on paper labels on spines: ‘THE / GOLDEN / LEGEND./ Vol. I. [11.] [11.]’ [Troy type]. PUBLICATION: 500 paper copies (5 gns.); no vellum copies. Issued 3 November 1892. NOTEs: The Golden Legend, a mediaeval collection of saints' lives, held great appeal for Morris. As Ellis succinctly phrased it in his ‘Memoranda': Among the books which serve to illustrate the religious life and mode of thought that prevailed in the middle ages, none holds a more important place than the “Legend Aurea."Compiled and put into form in the latter half of the 13th century, the welcome with which it was received by the clergy & the lettered world is attested by the great number of manuscripts of it which are still extant, while its ready reception by the people generally is evidenced by the fact that within a short time of its first appearance it was translated into the vulgar tongue of most of the nations of Europe, frequently with the hagiological preferences of the people of the country for which it was designed' (iii, 1282). It was intended to be thc earliest book produced at the KP, but because of its length and some problems with the first delivery of paper from Batchelor (see Ai), Morris was forced to issue several shorter volumes before it was completed. On 2ı July 189o, while the Golden type was still being cut, Morris purchased (from Sotheran, for $\mathcal { E } \mathfrak { s o } )$ (Sotheran to Cockerell, 30 November 1897 [BL C.102.h.18]) Wynkyn de Worde's 1527 reprint of Caxton's translation (now in NL); when Cockerell visited Kelmscott House on 14 August of that year, Morris read passages of it aloud to him, and Cockerell noted that ‘he thinks of reprinting [it] when his new type is all cast' (Cockerell diary). But Morris was uncertain whether to proceed with The Golden Legend or Caxton's History of Troy until Bernard Quaritch lent him a copy of the latter, which persuaded him that the G.L. is by far the most important book of the two; so’– he wrote Ellis on 29 August 189o – I accept your kind offer with many thanks indeed, and will begin printing as soon as the type is free from the Glittering Plain' (Mackail, ii, 262). In early September Ellis, Morris, and Quaritch were negotiating a contract (see Ei). Quaritch was eager to publish the book, because, he said, first I think every man of culture should be acquainted with the alleged lives of the Saints, and second I am aware of the philological value of all books Caxton' (Quaritch to Ellis, 8 September 189o [Bc]). Quaritch agreed to print 25o copies (a number almost immediately doubled) at his own expense, and Morris and Ellis were to contribute their services gratis in return for twelve copies each. But the contract, which is dated 1 September, stipulated that Morris was ‘to have absolute and sole control over choice of paper, choice of type, size of the reprint and selection of the printer'. Quaritch assumed that he would supply the paper, an idea that was abandoned after Morris met Joseph Batchelor later in the year. Quaritch was startled to discover that the only other modern reprint of The Golden Legend was an incomplete facsimile published by the Holbein Society in 1878 (which Morris owned [Library, no. 484]); however, the Early English Text Society had published several other Caxton titles during the 187os and 188os, and David Nutt, the London publisher, was issuing a limited edition of Caxton's Le Morte Darthur between 1889 and 1891. (For an account of the earlier English editions of The Golden Legend, see Pierce Butler, Legenda Aurea -Légende Dorée – Golden Legend: A Study of Caxton's Golden Legend . . . [Baltimore, 1899].) Since Morris did not own the Caxton edition (1483) of The Golden Legend (though he possessed at least ten other early editions by the time of his death), Ellis arranged to borrow it from the Cambridge University Library; his daughter Phyllis (afterwards Mrs. Payne) then transcribed it so that the kP printers would not have to handle the book itself; and later Ellis read proofs against the book. (W. H. Bowden reported that not one word of the transcription was illegible [Colebrook, p. 37]; and the surviving scraps of it [see a below] confirm this claim.) As usual, Morris and Ellis disagreed about how much editorial apparatus was needed: Ellis persuaded Edward Gordon Duff, the bibliographer, to prepare a list of English-language editions of The Golden Legend, but in the end Morris declined to include it. ‘The author-printer kicks much against introductions, and there is some practical difficulty as to bibliographical description in that there are but two sets of type[:] caps. for headings & that used for the text, Ellis explained to Duff on 20 March 1892 (HEH DF 268). Meanwhile, Morris wrestled with problems of design and typography: his initial view was that the book would have to be ‘some kind of folio', and he argued that Caxton's joined letters and contractions should not be reproduced, ‘since our book is to be a reprint, not a facsimile' (Morris to Ellis, 14 September 189o [Mackail, ii, 263–4]). (Morris also extensively altered Caxton's punctuation.) On 31 December he wrote to Ellis that the book could not be double column, since roman type is wider than blackletter (Mackail, ii, 26s-6). By March 1891 Morris was experimenting with trial pages (see k, l, and m below), on which he made endless small adjustments in order to secure the correct proportions of margins. Writing to Quaritch on 25 July 1891, Morris predicted that printing and binding would cost between £12oo and £14oo, and added: ‘Mr. Burne Jones has promised to design me a frontispiece for the book and for my part I feel sure there will be a moderate public who will like it very much'(BQ). Morris designed the border for page I and several of the initials while convalescing at Folkestone in April 1891 (Mackail, ii, 27o). The engraved title was cut by Hooper (invoice: HEH HM 366s4), as were most of the initials and borders, though a few of the earliest initials were cut by George Campfield. The first sheet was printed on 11 May 1891; the first volume, except for prelims and illustrations, was completed by October 1891. In September 1891 Burne-Jones saw some proofs and reported that hewent crazy with pleasure'(Memorials, ii, 217). ‘The two illustrations and the title (which was the first woodcut title designed by Morris) were not engraved until June and August, 1892, when the third volume was approaching completion'(List’). Both illustrations were touched up for the wood-engraver by Mr. Fairfax Murray in a photographic copy' (Mackail, ii, 293). In Morris's design for the engraved title (see d below), the vine ornament behind the lettering is much lighter than in the final version, and the last line reads ‘newly done'rather than ‘done anew'. Cockerell reports that about half a dozen'vellum proofs of the illustrations were printed (‘List'; see also q, r, and t below). Morris abandoned his plan to design a printer's mark incorporating a drawing of Kelmscott Manor (Mackail, ii, 293). The books were delivered to Quaritch from the binder by 4 October 1892 (Quaritch to B. Alfred Quaritch [Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, fol. 179]). Three days later Morris wrote to Quaritch: I must needs say that, looking at the finished book, I am proud of it, and of having pushed it through so promptly. It is just 2 years ago that Ellis & I began talking about doing it in this very house [Kelmscott Manor] where I am writing' (BQ). A receipt of the same date (Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 436, fol. 184) reveals that the total cost of printing and binding was £135o – within Morris's original estimate. The book was very favourably reviewed in The Times on 6 October (p. 4) and in the Library, 4 (1892), 346–7. On 28 October 1892, at the Blue Posts Restaurant, Cork Street, there was a dinner to celebrate the publication of The Golden Legend (menu and seating plan in MCL). The guests, in addition to Morris and Ellis, were Dr. F. J. Furnivall, Edward Gordon Duff, Joseph Knight, Dr. Richard Garnett, William Blaikie, William Michael Rossetti, Emery Walker, and C. F. Ellis. However, the publication of the book was marred by the first of several misunderstandings between Morris and Quaritch. In a letter to Walter T. Spencer, 14 November 1894, Cockerell later summarized the episode: ‘The facts of the case are as follows: the Golden Legend was published not by us, but by Mr. Quaritch, who on the day of publication raised the prices from £s.5.- to £6.6, and then by degrees to £1o.1o.-. The trade, being dissatisfied with the terms which Mr. Q. offered to supply them, did not push the book at the time, & the result was that a good many copies remained on Mr. Q's hands. These he is now attempting to sell at the original price £s.5.- to the public: with a discount of 15% to the trade'(LB). Ellis prepared a modern-spelling, seven-volume edition of The Golden Legend for J. M. Dent's ‘Temple Classics’' in 19oo. RELATED MATE RIALS: (a) Two leaves of MS (BL C.43.C.26 [ΣQQ44]). NOTEs: In the summer of 189o Morris was trying todecide which Caxton title to reprint in order to launch his new Press. . . . now I have borrowed a Recueil of the Histories of Troy (the Wynkyn de Worde of course) from Quaritch,’ he wrote to Èllis on 29 August, and have no doubt that the L.L. [Golden Legend] is by far the most important book of the two' (Mackail, ii, 262). Nevertheless, he eventually began printing the Recuyell – the first κP book in the Troy type and also the first in which the Chaucer type was employed (for prelims and end matter) – in February 1892. In a memorandum to Quaritch on 1 June 1892, Morris outlined his plans for the book: ‘Reprinted from the first edition of Caxton's Translation – will make a quarto about 7oo p.p. which will if possible be bound in one vol: the whole edition will be delivered to the publisher bound in vellum or marocco [sic] to my satisfaction, so as to harmonize with the typography. The book will be ornamented very richly with woodcut ornaments entirely designed by myself; no labour or expense will be spared on them and there will be much variety in them. The semi-Ġothic type designed by me, (with special regard to legibility) will appear in this book for the first time'(BQ). For Morris's contract with Quaritch, see E2. Morris supplied the following note on the book for Quaritch's catalogue: As to the matter of the the book, it makes a thoroughly amusing story, instinct with mėdiæval thought and manners. For though written at the end of the Middle Ages and dealing with classical mythology, it has in it no token of the coming “Renaissance" but is purely mediæval. It is the last issue of that story of Troy which had such a hold on men's imaginations; the story built up from a rumour of the Cyclic Poets, of the heroic City of Troy, defended by Priam, with his gallant sons, led by Hector the preux Chevalier, beset by the violent & brutal Greeks, who were looked on as the necessary machinery for bringing about the undeniable tragedy of the fall of the city. Surely this is well worth reading, if only as a piece of undiluted Mediævalism’(see a below). There are several references to the book's progress in Cockerell's diary. 21 April 1892: ‘Took a sheet of the blackletter Recuyell to send to [John Ruskin at] Brantwood.'2 June 1892: Morris had several new side borders for the Recuyell etc.’28 July 1892: Morris had pages of Recuyell experimentally illuminated by [Edmond] Reuter.'6 September 1892: ‘Saw . . . pages of the Recuyell (68o now printed).’ The block for the engraved title was cut by C. E. Keates (Keates's bill, 3 October 1892: HEH HM 36661). Altogether Quaritch paid Morris £1o12 1os. for the printing of the volume (receipts of 25 November 1892 and 28 February 1893 [BQ]). The Star (London), 21 November 1892, p. 2, offered a vivid account of a pre-publication celebration at Kelmscott House: Mr. William Morris . . . showed to a few friends on Tuesday last an advance copy of his reprint of Caxton's “Recueil of the Historyes of Troye." On looking through it, Dr. Furnivall said enthusiastically, “It's the most beautiful book I ever saw; it's the most beautiful book ever printed!" Mr. F. S. Ellis . . . cordially agreed in this opinion, and so did Mr. Emery Walker . . . and Mr. Theodore Watts. . . . Though there had been a number of earlier editions of the Recuyell, the KP version was the first to go back directly to Caxton's text. Nevertheless, H. Oskar Sommer, in an edition of the Recuyell published by David Nutt in 1894, made a strong attack upon the textual reliability of the KP edition: In our own days . . . Mr. William Morris has produced . . . an edition of“The Recuyell," which, as regards getting up and artistic execution, is all that can be desired; but from my point of view, that of the critic and philologist, it is absolutely without any value. I have often wondered how it is that Mr. Morris, who is so rich in resources and designs, cannot find the means for the production of a beautiful book which at the same time meets all the requirements of modern scholarship. If Mr. Morris was determined to make the Trojan War the subject of one of his beautiful books, why did he not select Lydgate's Troy-Book, so infinitely superior to “The Recuyell," which from a purely literary point of view, has no value whatever?' Of the Recuyell, Reynard the Foxe (Aro), and Godefrey of Boloyne (Aı5), Sparling says: . . . Morris wished them to be regarded as Kelmscott Press editions, and therefore to be amended where this was desirable. Caxton's text was to be taken as a basis, but not looked upon as archaeologically sacrosanct. It was to be collated with Caxton's originals, and corrected where need was, mistranslations being put right and omissions filled in, care being taken to preserve the style and flavour of Caxton in doing this'(p. 84). The Kelmscott edition was to have been illustrated with approximately two hundred wood-engravings designed by Burne-Jones, of which twenty-five designs were completed. These – as redrawn by Robert Catterson-Smith - were published in The Beginning of the World: Twenty-Five Pictures by Edward Burne-Jones (1902). The fact / of being able to study these poems in the language / in which they were written, ought we think, to add / materially to the pleasure of the reader. Printing of the frontispiece – which was engraved by Hooper (HE H HM 3665s) – was completed on 7 March 1893 (Morris diary). News from Nowhere was the last κP book, except for Epistola de Contemptu Mundi (A3ı), to use paragraph symbols. ENGRAVED TITLE: THE / ORDER OF / CHIVALRY [gothic lettering designed by Morris] CoLoPHoN (h3v): The Order of Chivalry, translated from / the French by William Caxton, edited by / F. S. Ellis, & printed by me William Morris / at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Ham-/mersmith, in the County of Middlesex, & / finished on the ioth day of November, 1892 / [paragraph] Sold by Reeves & Turner, 196, Strand, / London. / [printer's mark 1] HALF-TITLE (i): L'ORDENE DE CHEVALERIE, WITH / TRANSLATION BY WILLIAM MORRIS ## HALF-TITLE (k4): THE ORDINATION OF KNIGHTHOOD. COLOPHON (18r): THIS Ordination of Knighthood was / printed by William Morris at the Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, / in the County of Middlesex; finished on / the 24th day of February, 1893. / [printer's mark 1] EXTRACT FROM ELLIS'S MEMORANDA': [16V] THE “Order of Chivalry" was translated by Caxton from the French original, of which there are many early manuscripts in existence. . . . [paragraph] A strange confusion has been made by various writers & bibliographers between this treatise and a charming little French poem of the 13th century entitled “L’Ordene de Chevalerie." This was first printed at Paris in 1759, by M. Barbazan, and again in the “Fabliaux et Contes," Paris, 1808, from the text found in the MS. volume 25462, fo. 149–157, in the National Library at Paris. . . . [l7r] The poem is here reprinted from the text given in the “Fabliaux et Contes,"1808. . . . As the poem is ascribed to the 13th century, and the learned Director of the French National Library attributes the prose work to the 14th century, it might very well be that the author of the “Ordre de Chevalerie" was acquainted with the earlier work & might have been in some measure inspired by it. . . . [18r] The interest that it [The Order of Chivalry'] has now as an historical document is considerable, and the wonder is that it has not been reprinted before this time in our own days. COLLTION: Smal1 40: [a]² b-g⁸ h4. 80: i–18. 78 leaves, pP. [4][1] 2-150 [151-2]. PAPER & DIMENSIONs: Flower(1) ([a]-h) and Flower(2) (i-l) paper. Leaf: 200 × 138 mm. Text: 28 lines × 88 mm. TYPE: Chaucer type. ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATION: Borders 9a and 4; corner borders; three-, six-, and ten-line initials; chapter titles in ‘The Order of Chivalry' in red; wood-engraved frontispiece designed by Burne-Jones. CONTENTs: [a]ı title [a]1-[a]2r list of chapters [a4]2 frontispiece and engraved title br-h3 text of The Order of Chivalry h3 colophon h4r-h4 blank iır half-title iı blank i2r–k3r text of L'Ordene de Chevalerie l6v–18r ‘MEMORANDA CONCERNING THE / TWO PIECES HERE REPRINTED.'(by Ellis) l8r ¢olophon 18v blank. BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘THE ORDER OF CHIVALRY' [Troy type]. PUBLICATION: 225 paper copies (3os.); 10 vellum copies (10 gns.) Issued 12 April 1893. NOTEs: There are frequent allusions to The Order of Chivalry in Cockerell's diary. 12 November 1892: ‘Saw the sheets of Caxton's Order of Chivalry the printing of which is just complete. It is the prettiest book yet done.'3 December 1892: ‘w.M. began the translation of a French poem from which Caxton seems to have derived the idea of the Order of Chivalry. It was wonderful to watch the words slipping into their places.' 27 January 1893: w.M. translating L’ordene de Chivalerie.'31 January 1893: w.M. experimenting with lettering for the Order of Chivalry cut.'13 February 1893: w.M. doing a border for the “Order of Chivalry"illustration.’2 March 1893: Morris showed Cockerell proofs of the illustrations for the Order of Chivalry'. The inclusion of L'Ordene was an afterthought; hence the book's peculiar format. (The first part of the volume represents the final use of the smaller Flower paper.) L'Ordene was initially translated into prose by Ellis before Morris decided to render it in verse (Mackail, ii, 295). It was the first book printed in Chaucer type. The reprint . . . was finished while News from Nowhere [A12] was in the press, and before Shakespeare's Poems and Sonnets [A11] was begun'(List’). On 14 February 1893 Morris delivered the border to Hooper (Morris diary), who also engraved the frontispiece (Hooper's bill, 27 February 1893: HEH HM 36654). Some of the three-line initials were designed for The Well at the World's End (A39) but were used here for the first time. For a more recent scholarly edition, see The Book of the Ordre of Chyvalry, ed. Alfred T. P. Byles (Early English Text Society, 1926). COLOPHON: Transcribed after the autograph manuscript of the / author, now in the British Museum, by F. S. Ellis, / and finished on the 2sth day of December, in the year / 1892, in the Parish of Cockington in the County / of Devon. And printed by me William Morris, / at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Hammer-/smith in the County of Middlesex, and finished / on the 3oth day of March, 1893. / [printer's mark 1] / Sold by Reeves & Turner, 196, Strand. FOREwORD: THE author of this life of Cardinal / Wolsey held the Office of gentil-/man usher in his household from / 1526 till 153o. The book was writ-/ten about 1557. It was not printed / till nearly a hundred years later, but / was well known through the many manuscript / copies of it which were handed about, all of which / however were more or less garbled & incomplete. / From one of these imperfect copies an edition was / printed in 164ı, & several other editions, all more / or less incomplete followed from time to time. At / the beginning of the present century Mrs. S. W. / Singer had the good fortune to light upon the au-/tograph MS. of the author, from which he printed / an edition prefaced by an elaborate introduction / and supplemented by very useful notes and ap-/pendices. He thought well however to modernize / the author's spelling, and occasionally to endeav-/our to clear or improve the style by slight altera-/tions. In a few instances the author's words were / inadvertently changed. The book is therefore now / for the first time put before the reader in the exact / form in which it was left by the author. One spe-/cial point of interest about it is, that it presents us / with the first separate biography in the English / language. But for it, we should have but a very imperfect idea of one of the most remarkable / figures in English history. Chroniclers, historians, / and dramatists have alike been indebted to it in / writing of Wolsey. COLLATION: 80: [a]4 b-t8. 148 leaves, PP. [4] [i] ii-iv [=8] [1] 2-287 [288]. NOTEs: The Ms which Singer used as the basis of his edition (2 vols., 1825) is BL Egerton 2402, which was acquired by the British Museum in 1876. ENGRAVED TITLE: this is the / history of Godefrey / of Boloyne / and of the / conquest of Iherusalem [gothic lettering designed by Morris] COLOPHON: THIS new edition of William Caxton's God-/effroy of Boloyne, done after the first edition, was / corrected for the press by H. Halliday Sparling, / and printed by me, William Morris, at the Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the / County of Middlesex, & finished on the 27th day / of April, 1893. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press. COLLATION: Large 40: π8 [a]4 b−2f8 2g2. 238 leaves, pp. [i-ij] iij-xxij [2] [=24] [1] 2-450 [451-2]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 287 × 205 mm. Text: 32-3 lines × 126 mm. CONTENTs: π title πI blank π2-π5 foreword (by Caxton) π5ν- [a]3 table of contents [a]4 blank [a]4 engraved title brr-2gı text 2gi Caxton's colophon 2g2 glossary 2g2 colophon. BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: GODEFREY/ OF / BOLOYNE' [enlarged Troy type]. PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (6 gns.); 6 vellum copies (2o gns.). Issued 24 May 1893. NOTEs: The following note in the announcement (C5) of Godefrey was written by Morris: ‘This book must from a literary point of view be considered one of Caxton's most important works. It is not a “romance," as it has been called by Dibdin, Blades, and others, but a very serious piece of history, the original of which is William of Tyre's Historia Rerum in partibus transmarinis Gestarum; from the French translation of which Caxton made his English version, bringing it to an end just after the death of Godfrey, whereas the Archbishop of Tyre carries on the history for many years after. The style of Caxton's book is vigorous and agreeable, and often reads like the most dramatic of all historic works, Lord Berner's translation of Froissart, and altogether it is a delightful book to read. ![](images/a194e2b825da9ecb4b51f671d7aadae9672445ab8bfeb4ef9e2a9183c33098b4.jpg) 8 The engraved title (reduced) of The History of Godefrey of Boloyne (Aı5), designed by Morris. There are many new ornaments in this the latest issue from the Kelmscott Press; for the chapters being very short and the scheme of ornament being very similar to that of the last two quartos, the Reynard and the Recuyell, the book is very decorative. The book does indeed contain a profusion of ornaments, many of which were engraved by Keates (see h below). The larger printer's mark was also used for the first time. In the proofs (see f below) there are numerous notes from Morris to his printers about the ornaments: e.g., ‘½ border 1o [–] if it will not come in ½ border 9. but get it in if you can'(p. 341) and ½ border 5 - but take care it does not print heavy'(p. 243). Quaritch was very annoyed by Morris's decision to publish this, the last of the five Caxton reprints, himself: Mr. W. Morris wishes to have all the profits of the Kelmscott press; he will retail himself the [’]Godfrey of Bouloyne" – he will be a loser by it, but it will be a nuisance to me on account of the Chaucer'(Quaritch to B. Alfred Quaritch, 8 February 1893 [Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 189]). Quaritch's prediction was accurate, for Godefrey did not sell well, and in 1897 Mrs. Morris donated a number of copies to public libraries. Morris showed the first copy of Godefrey to Cockerell and others on the evening of 11 May 1893 (Cockerell diary). NOTEs: The suggestion to print More's Utopia apparently originated with Cockerell on 29 February 1892 (Cockerell diary). The book was printed during July and early August 1893, and the cost of production was £255 14s. 9d. (Ledger). Morris wrote the foreword on 12 July 1893 (Morris to Jenny Morris, 13 July 1893 [BL Add. Ms. 45340, f. 122]). The first English edition of Utopia was published in 1551 and the second edition (which Morris owned [Library, no. $6 4 7 ] ) \textrm { - }$ newlie perused and corrected, and also with divers notes in the margent augmented' – in 1556. The printer of both editions was Abraham Vele. In a presentation copy of the KP volume, Ellis wrote: I cannot help feeling vexed at my name appearing as Editor opposite a palpable misprint $[ \mathrm { } ^ { \bullet \bullet } \mathrm { \overline { { W } } e a l e } ^ { \prime \bullet } ]$ of the first printer's name. I did correct it, but my proof was lost and the misprint left' (see o below). Robinson's translation had been reprinted several times during the nineteenth century. Morris at one time intended to reproduce a map of Utopia that appeared in the first Latin edition of the work (see c below). Aymer Vallance (William Morris [1897], p. 40o) records an amusing anecdote: ‘Of the 3oo copies issued, 4o had been ordered in advance by an Eton master, with the intention of distributing them as prizes among the boys of the college, but when the work appeared with a compromisingly Socialistic introduction by Morris, the order, from motives of pruderce, had to be cancelled. However the copies were all disposed of before a year was out, so Morris did not suffer any loss.' NOTEs: Morris owned at least four volumes of Tennyson's poetry while an Oxford undergraduate: Poems, 1oth ed. $\left( \Im \ S \ - s \right)$ , In Memoriam, 6th ed. $\left( { \tt I 8 5 5 } \right)$ , Maud, and Other Poems $\left( { \tt I 8 5 5 } \right)$ , and The Princess, 6th ed. $\left( 1 8 5 4 \right)$ (all now at κM). After the publication of the κP edition of Maud, Morris confessed to Blunt: I would sooner . . . have printed them [Macmillan & Co.] Tennyson's first volume, which is all I ever cared for in his poems'(Blunt diary, 6 October 1893). On a later occasion Morris told Blunt that Tennyson ‘never forgave him and Burne-Jones for having disapproved of his bowdlerization of the Morte d’Arthur in the “Idylls of the $\mathrm { K i n g } ^ { \prime \prime }$ (Blunt diary, 29 May 1896). Maud, which was commissioned by Macmillan $\& \ \subset \mathbf { o } .$ ,， made a handsome profit for Morris: his production cost (including vellum copies) was $\mathcal { f } _ { 2 0 2 } \ \mathbf { I } _ { 4 } s .$ 1d. (Ledger), and for the 5oo paper copies delivered to Macmillan he charged £,4oo (see E5). 'The borders were specially designed for this book. They were both used again in the Keats [A24], and one of them appears in The Sundering Flood $\left[ \mathrm { A } \varsigma \mathrm { I } \right] ^ { 3 } \left( ^ { \circ } \mathrm { L i s t } ^ { 3 } \right)$ On 14 November 1893 Bertram Dobell, the bookseller, wrote to Macmillan that a customer had returned his copy of Maud complaining of typographical errors on pp. 19, 26, and 69. Two days later Morris replied to Macmillan: I am sorry to say that as to the places quoted in Mr. Dobell's note, the facts are as stated; but having been very carefully over the book I can see no other errors. I will go over it again and when I have found out what the worst is, we will consider what is best to be done'(HEH HM 36673). As a result, four cancellans leaves were printed $\left( \mathrm { p p . ~ I 5 - I 6 , ~ I 9 - 2 0 , ~ } 2 . 5 \mathrm { - } 6 , 6 \mathrm { - } 7 0 \right)$ and were perhaps inserted in at least a few copies of Maud. The errors corrected were as follows: p. 16, 1. 2o, ‘edge'should be head'; p. 19, 1. 4, ‘to' should be from'; p. 26, 1. 18, ‘where’should be ‘were'; p. 69, l. I, and war should be‘and the war'. COLOPHoN: This paper, first spoken as a lec-/ture at the New Gallery, for the / Arts & Crafts Exhibition Society, / in the year 1889, was printed by the Kelmscott Press during the Arts / and Crafts Exhibition at the New / Gallery, Regent Street, London, / 1893. / [paragraph] Sold by William Morris, Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammer-/smith. COLLATION: 160: a⁸(a8 + π) b−d⁸ e4. 37 leaves, PP. [2] 1-68 [4] [=72]. PAPER & DIMENSIONs: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 143× I04 mm. Text: 19 lines $\times 6 5$ mm. TYPE: Golden type. Printed during October and November 1893. First copies issued 21 October 1893. NOTEs: Work on the book began in late August 1893 (Ledger). During October and November a press was moved to the New Gallery for the annual Arts and Crafts Exhibition, and Gothic Architecture was printed in public, under the eyes of an interested and constantly renewed crowd, whose presence imposed a severe strain upon the pressman Collins's Celtic modesty' (Sparling, p. 87). There were three impressions, presumably of soo copies each (though Cockerell, in a letter to Charles F. Carter of 8 February 1895, sets the total of paper copies at 154o [LB]): in the first impression‘Van Eyck'(p. 45, 1. 1) was misspelled ‘Van Eyk’, and ‘guilds'(p. 41, 1. 16) was misspelled ‘gilds’. The second-state readings are present in both the second and third impressions and the vellum copies. This was the first book printed in 16° at the KP. At least one trial page was done in the Chaucer type (see e below).‘The four-line initials used in it appear here for the first time'(List’). Cost of production was £165 18s. 5d. (Ledger). (o) Copy (vellum) with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (Parke-Bernet cat., 5 November 1946, no. 313). ## A19 William Meinhold, Sidonia the Sorceress SIDONIA THE SORCERESS [ornament 1] BY WILLIAM / MEINHOLD TRANSLATED BY FRANCESCA / SPE-RANZA LADY WILDE. COLoPHoN: Here ends the story of Sidonia the Sorceress, translated from the / German of William Meinhold, by Francesca Speranza, Lady / Wilde, and now reprinted by me, William Morris, at the Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of Middle-/sex. Finished on the 15th day of September, 1893. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press. COLLATION: Large 40: [a]8 b−2f8 2g4. 236 leaves, pp. [2] [i-ii] iii-xiv [=16] [1] 2-455 [456]. PAPER & DIMENSIONs: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 287×205 mm. Text: 41 lines × 126 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTS: Border 8; six- and ten-line initials; chapter titles, speakers’ names, and side notes in red. CONTENTs: [a]-[a]1ν blank [a]2r title [a]2ν blank [a]3r–[a]5r preface (by Meinhold) [a]5 epistle from Theodore Plonnies to Bogislaff the Fourteenth, the last Duke of Pomerania [a]6–[a]8 table of contents bīr-2g4 text 2g4 colophon. BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘SI-DONIA / THE / SORCERESS' [Troy tγpe]. PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (4 gns.); 10 vellum copies (20 gns.) Issued 1 November 1893. NOTES: Morris wrote the following note for the announcement (C9) of Sidonia: SIDONIA THE SORCERESS, the translation of which is now on the point of being issued by the Kelmscott Press, is an Historical Romance, based more or less on fact, concerning the Witch Fever that afflicted Northern Europe during the latter half of the ith & first half of the 16th centuries. It was written by William Meinhold, a Lutheran minister, dwelling in the island of Rugen, off the shore of Pomerania, a man so steeped in the history and social life of his country during the period above-mentioned, that he might almost be said to have been living in it, rather than in his own, the early part of the present century. The result of his life and literary genius was the production of two books: “The Amber Witch," & “Sidonia the Sorceress," both of which, but, in my judgment, especially “Sidonia," are almost faultless reproductions of the life of the past; not mere antiquarian studies, but presentations of events, often tragic, the actors in which are really alive, though under conditions so different from those of the present day. In short, “Sidonia"is a masterpiece of its kind, and without a rival of its kind. It must be added that it was a great favourite with the more literary part of the pre-Raphaelite artists in the earlier days of that movement. The two beautiful water-colour pictures of Sidonia and of Clara von Dewitz, by Mr. Burne-Jones, which the general public had the opportunity of seeing at the exhibition of his pictures this spring, caused many questions to be asked about this, which the present edition of the book will answer satisfactorily. Lady Wilde's translation, which was the one through which we made acquaintance with Meinhold's genius, is a good, simple, and sympathetic one.' Lady Wilde's translation of Sidonia the Sorceress, which was published in the Parlour Library in 1849, was well known to Morris's circle: Rossetti in particular was an enthusiastic admirer of it, and Burne-Jones produced two water-colours based on the story in 186o. Oscar Wilde's mother doubtless appreciated Morris's cheque for £25 (see d below), for she was living in extreme poverty in the 189os. Through the intervention of Aymer Vallance, Aubrey Beardsley prepared a drawing of Sidonia intended as a frontispiece, which he showed to Morris in the spring or early summer of 1892. Morris's comments, though he praised Beardsley's feeling for draperies', were so discouraging that the young artist abandoned the attempt, and thereafter Beardsley expressed great hostility towards Morris and the κP (Vallance, ‘The Invention of Aubrey Beardsley', Magazine of Art, May 1898, pp. 363-4; Vallance, ‘List of Drawings by Aubrey Beardsley', in Robert Ross, Aubrey Beardsley [1921], p. 71). Work began on Sidonia in the first half of 1893 (Ledger); on 13 April 1893 Morris recorded in his diary: I have now got 2½ sections of Sidonia printed.' Cost of production was £s84 18s. (Ledger). The book did not sell briskly: on 1o October 1894 Cockerell instructed Leighton to bind thirty copies ‘in half holland uniform with the Golden Legend'(LB), and these were later donated to British and American libraries. After Sidonia's publication, F. S. Ellis wrote to Morris on 6 January 1894 listing the printer's errors he had found in it: I have just finished reading aloud “Sidonia"& am more impressed than ever with its excellence. . . . To come to the point there are certainly more misprints than there ought to be. Pray dont think that I consider myself perfect as a press-reader, far from it & it is a consciousness of the difficulty of one man getting a thing perfect that makes me propose that you should send me a proof simultaneously with your other reader, simply on the principle that four eyes are better than two. In case you have not had your attention called to what I mean I will be the “damned good natured friend"[.] p. 195 (1. 1), “rings" for “wrings” p. 35o note 1. 3 “Heredotus” p. 355 Šide note. 1. 8. “Silvertre" for “Silvestre” p. 415. 1. 27 “navitate" for “nativitate” p. 419. 1. 15 “coach" for “couch” p. 437. 1. 9 “hert" for “her" p. 441. 1. 28. “morning” for “mourning”. These are but a few that I have noted, for when I had no penci at hand I didn't mark ’em. The same (in a less degree) is the case with “Godfrey". Perhaps I am one of the few people who have read all the books you have printed' (BL Add. Ms. 52715, f. 11). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Trial pages (BC). (b) Inc. corrected (in part by Morris) proofs (BL C.43.f.24 [∆bīo]). (c) Corrected proofs of pp. 96, 113, 129, 161 (HEH Morrisiana 12’). (d) Pres. copy to Cockerell, 22 January 1894, with Ms in Morris's hand of his note for the prospectus, also a letter to Morris from Lady Wilde respecting the use of her translation for this edition, and a receipt, signed by her, for £25 “for the new edition of Sidonia," 23 Jan. 1893' (Cockerell sale, no. 22). ENGRAVED TITLE: BALLADS / AND /NARRATIVE / POEMS BY / DANTE / GABRIEL / ROSSETTI [roman lettering designed by Morris] CoLoPHoN: [ornament 1] Here ends the Book of Ballads and Narrative / Poems, written by Dante Gabriel Rossetti, and / printed by William Morris at the Kelmscott / Press, 14 Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the / County of Middlesex; finished on the 14th day / of October, of the year 1893. / [printer's mark 1] / Published by Ellis & Elvey, 29 New Bond Street. COLLATION: 80: [a]4 b-p8 q4. 120 leaves, PP. [8] [1] 2-227 [228] [4] [=232]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 205 × I40 mm. Text: 27–31 lines × 92 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTs: Borders 4a and 4; six- and ten-line initials; some lines and shoulder titles in red. CONTENTS: $\ [ \mathsf { a } ] _ { } \mathbf { I } ^ { \mathsf { r } } - \bigl [ \mathsf { a } \bigr ] \mathsf { 2 ^ { v } }$ blank [a]3r title [a]3 table of contents [a]4 blank [a]4 engraved title bīr–q1 text q2 colophon q2-q4 blank. BinDiNG: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘BALLADS/AND /POEMS/BY/D.G./ROSSETTI'[reduced Golden type]. PUBLICATION: 310 paper copies (2 gns.); 6 vellum copies (10 gns.). Issued November 1893. NOTES: In the Ellis papers at UCLA there are frequent references to this and its companion volume, Sonnets and Lyrical Poems by Dante Gabriel Rossetti (A2oa). Early in July 1893 Morris agreed to print Rossetti's poems for Ellis & Elvey, recommending that they be divided into two volumes and that his charge per copy (for each volume) would be 2ıs. (see E6). By 12 July Ellis & Elvey reported to William Michael Rossetti, who held his brother's literary rights, that the first volume – which Morris suggested calling Ballads and Tales in Verse – would be ready by the end of November. The title was slightly altered at the request of W. M. Rossetti, who read proofs and carefully supervised the book. One issue that arose during the printing of Sonnets and Lyrical Poems was whether the sonnet Nuptial Sleep'should be restored to The House of Life. D. G. Rossetti had deleted the sonnet in 1872 after it was brutally attacked by Robert Buchanan in his notorious essay ‘The Fleshly School of Poetry', and William Michael, in a preface to his brother's Collected Works (1886), explained that ‘his own decision commands mine'(i, xxxiii). W. M. Rossetti again declined to allow the sonnet to appear in the KP edition; he restored it to the text of The House of Life only in 1904. W. M. Rossetti submitted the table of contents for the second volume in early November 1893, and on 27 December Ellis & Elvey sent him proofs of the first sheet. The KP Ledger is not entirely clear in distributing costs between the two volumes, but it appears that the production cost of Sonnets and Lyrical Poems was £2o2 5s. On 6 July 1894 Ellis & Elvey wrote to W. M. Rossetti: ‘The Kelmscott Press Editions have we think done fairly well [during the first half of the year]. 264 copies of vol ı & 265 of vol ı1 have been sold altogether.' Meanwhile, in America, Copeland & Day had early in 1894 produced an edition of The House of Life, done in close imitation of the Kelmscott style, which troubled both Morris and Ellis & Elvey. However much we may disapprove of this publication, & the bad imitation of the Kelmscott Press we fear we cannot help it,’Ellis & Elvey declared to Rossetti on 1 February 1894. (Copeland & Day had sent a copy of the book to Ellis & Elvey.) RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Proof of p. I (PML ‘Morrisiana II’). (b) Pres. copy to Cockerell, 3 February 1894, with original designs by Morris for eight initials (five in an early state) and a letter from Sir E. Burne-Jones to Sir Sydney Cockerell asking for a copy of the Poems' (Cockerell sale, no. 23). (c) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 17 December 1893 (PM L). (d) Pres. copy to Philip Webb, 14 December 1893 (τC). (e) Pres. copy to Jenny Morris (Fm[1]). (f) Harry Buxton Forman's copy (BC). (g) Pres. copy to Kate Faulkner, 14 December 1893 (Parke-Bernet cat., 26 November 1975, no. 742). (h) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 29 December 1893 (Sotheby cat., 22 December 1952, no. 57). (i) Copy (vellum) with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: ‘Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897'(PcL). (j) Pres. copy to Robert W. Smith, 23 January 1894 (Merwin-Clayton Sales Company [New York] cat., 4 March 1912, no. 374). ## A2oa Dante Gabriel Rossetti, Sonnets and Lyrical Poems SONNETS AND LYRICAL POEMS / BY DANTE GABRIEL ROSSETTI. NOTES: The source of this and two other KP books – Of the Friendship of Amis and Amile (A23) and The Tale of the Emperor Coustans and of Over Sea (A26) – was Nouvelles françaises en prose du xiiième siècle by L. Moland and C. D'Hericault (Paris, 1856). Morris's translations were later collected in a single volume entitled Old French Romances Done into English by William Morris, published by George Allen in 1896 with an introduction by Joseph Jacobs, who described the tales ‘as a sort of outhouse to that goodly fabric so appropriately known to us all as The Earthly Paradise'(p. xxv). I daresay you will despise some of my queries & questions. Cost of production was £71 16s. 1d. (Ledger). Seventy-six sets of sheets of King Florus were distributed by Tregaskis to the world's leading binders, and the results were exhibited in London between 27 June and 7 July 1894. (See the Studio, 3 [July 1894], 125-6, and A Memorable Exhibit of Bookbinding', British Printer, 7 [July-August 1894], 13.) These copies are now in the John Rylands Library, Manchester. Morris was so eager to get the first edition (Ai) into print that he would not wait for Crane's illustrations; but later (probably in the autumn of 1892) Morris again approached Crane about illustrating a new edition (see F3), with the profits from the book to be split between them. Crane earned at least £Iso for his contribution to the book (Cockerell to A. J. R. Gooderson, 27 September 1894 [L B]). Crane's designs were cut on wood by A. Leverett. I think your woodcuts look delightful,' Morris wrote to Crane on 17 February 1894 (letter privately printed by William P. Barlow, Jr., 1975). Crane himself, however, doubted ‘if I was ever quite Gothic enough in feeling to suit his [Morris's] taste' (The Work of Walter Crane’, Art Journal, Easter Annual, 1898, p. 10). Philip Webb, in a letter to Morris, 18 February 1894, commented: ‘The book is quite up to the Thames-side mark in all but the pictures, and these, if one is careful in looking, have their value: but Crane will not (or can-not) take pains. A lot of work in these blocks is good – really good – but the inaptitudes are abundant. . . . However, do not mind – the whole book looks delightful; and, for the pictures . . . why, they are more than helped out by the borders, and the heavyline engraving of the subjects. But the features; why, they are killed by Crane's no-notion of surface, & of black[,] white and grey. Also, costume is before the figure it clothes. I cannot see the difficulty with a knowledge of old time – of inventing costume. Alack – but still, it don't much matter; but, again: do not be so rash in trusting the picture makers in future. How can they know?'(v & A[1] 86.TT.3). Cockerell later added this note to Webb's letter: ‘Morris was no less dissatisfied than Webb with Crane's illustrations to his Glittering Plain & thought this volume his one Kelmscott Press failure. Morris designed the borders in late 1892 and early 1893 (Cockerell diary, 1 November 1892; Morris diary, 18 March 1893). ‘Neither the borders in this book nor six out of the seven frames round the illustrations appear in any other book. The seventh is used round the second picture in Love is Enough [A52]’(List’). The engraved title was cut by Hooper (Hooper's bill, 15 December 1893: HEí M 36655). The copytext was not the earlier KP edition but the edition printed in 1891 at the Chiswick Press (see c below). The names of the following compositors appear in the marked text: H. H.[owes], Binning, Tippett, and Collins. The cost of production was £485 18s. 8d. (Ledger). The book sold slowly, and on i1 October 1894 Cockerell instructed Leighton to ‘bind up the 31 unbound Glittering Plains in half holland . . .' (LB). BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘THE POEMŚ / OF / JOHN KEATS.' [Ġolden type]. PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (3os.); 7 vellum copies (9 gns.). Issued 8 May 1894. NOTEs: On 25 May 1893 Ellis wrote to Harry Buxton Forman: Morris is printing at the Kelmscott Press a small edition of certain Poems of Keats – al those which he considers worth printing & I am seeing it through the press for him. ‘Would you mind telling me if any of Keats' Poems are at present copyright, as he would of course omit them. It will simply [be] a handsomely printed volume without notes of any kind' (BC). Sheet y had to be reprinted because the wrong version of Keats's La Belle Dame sans Merci’was used. A slightly garbled text of the poem had appeared in Leigh Hunt's Indicator in 182o and was occasionally reprinted thereafter, and it was this inferior version which had been set up by the KP compositors. Cockerell records Morris's reaction: In February 1894 the last sheets of the Kelmscott Press Keats . . . were being printed. A specimen of each sheet of every book was brought in to Morris as soon as it came off the press. I was with him when he happened to open the sheet on which La Belle Dame sans Merci was printed. He began to read it and was suddenly aware of unfamiliar words. . . . Great was his indignation. He swiftly altered the words and then read the poem to me, remarking that it was the germ from which all the poetry of his group had sprung – The sheet was reprinted and the earlier and better version restored - I still have the cancelled sheet with his corrections'(Sidney Colvin, John Keats: His Life and Poetry, His Friends, Critics and After-Fame [1918], p. 470). As an undergraduate Morris owned the 1854 edition of Keats’s Poetical Works (now at km), and his own poetry, as he admitted, shows indebtedness to Keats (see Clarice Short, William Morris and Keats’, PMLA, 59 [June, 1944], 513–23). Cockerell saw the first page of the KP Keats on 8 October 1893 (diary). Cost of production was £372 8s. 1od. (Ledger). The book was popular and went out of print very quickly. Macmillan & Co., who have kindly / allowed them to be used here./ [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press. COLLATION: Large 4º: [a]⁴ b-f⁸ g⁴. 46 leaves, pp. [8] [1] 2-81 [82-4]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Perch paper. Leaf: 289 × 2I0 mm. Text: 31 lines × 130 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTs: Borders 5a and 5; side and three-quarter borders; three-, six-, eight-, and eleven-line initials; row of ornaments on [a]2ν and speakers’ names (usually in margin) in red. CoNTENTs: [a]ı title and Greek epigraph [a]ı-[a]2ν dedication to Walter Savage Landor and poem in Greek [a]3r dramatis personæ and quotation from AEschylus [a]3 argument [a]4 blank [a]4 engraved title bır–gı text gı colophon g2r-g4 blank. BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘ATALANTA. SWINBURNE'[Golden type; second word reduced]. PUBLICATION: 250 paper copies (2 gns.); 8 vellum copies (12 gns.). Issued 24 July 1894. NOTEs: Swinburne was a warm admirer of the KP books, and he and Morris maintained a friendly but not intimate relationship. Wilfrid Scawen Blunt recorded in his diary on 26 May 1896: ‘Swinburne's new poem was reviewed yesterday in all the papers. Morris thinks it poor stuff and not worth doing as the story Balin and Balan was quite perfect in its prose form in the Morte d’Arthur. “It will not do however" he said “for Swinburne to hear me saying this, for he would never forgive me". Swinburne, it appears[,] is the most sensitive and jealous of men and cannot bear the smallest criticism, but he and Morris have not met for years though Mrs. Morris goes now and then to see Swinburne.'When Atalanta (which was first published in 1865) was printed by Morris, Swinburne wrote to him that it was certainly one of the loveliest examples of even your incomparable press'(John Y. LeBourgeois, ‘Some Unpublished letters of Swinburne', Notes and Queries, NS 19 [July 1972], 262). Morris's own comment, in a letter to Theodore Watts, 9 August 1894, was ‘I like the look of the volume very much'(CNY). This is the only κP book in which Morris used a type not designed by himself. During 1892 Selwyn Image, with the technical assistance of Emery Walker, designed a Greek type based ‘on a certain tenth century Ms in the B. Museum, and on a Spanish printed book there of the fifteenth or early sixteenth century, which was itself founded on a Ms of the 1oth century'(Seluyn Image Letters, ed. A. H. Macmurdo [1932], p. 75). A series of letters from Image to Macmillan & Co. about the designing of the type is in BL Add. Ms. 5526o. The type was first used in Archer Hind's edition of the Phædo (1894). On 27 April 1894 Morris wrote to Macmillan: . . . I venture to ask you to be kind enough to lend me some of your new Greek type for the quotations & dedicatory poems. In the event of your not wishing the type to leave your hands, the passages might perhaps be set up by you & stereotyped – in which case I may mention that I propose to have them in caps throughout, without leading or indentation (BL Add. Ms. 5526o, f. 176). In the end, Macmillan supplied electrotypes. In the vellum copies of Atalanta, the colophon appears on g2r. Cost of production was £27o 1os. 6d. (Ledger). The book was sold out within à few weeks of publication.
