The book was printed during the spring of 1894 but was not issued until the following autumn because of a delay in completing the frontispiece (Ledger; Sparling, p. 79). ![](images/f8627c8b1373674b6f33159ce8917d18f8465400178af6722da90cc99700eb71.jpg) 11 The frontispiece (reduced) of The Wood beyond the World (A27), designed by Edward Burne-Jones, with the border designed by Morris. Of Golden alter and hís father ![](images/db38d619d72ba840d5844cd83f8dd81db5611b4072981141f447506ce5517326.jpg) WHILE AGO τ∈R∈ 飄AS HYOANGMAN DELLINGIN HGREHTHND goodly city by the sea which had tonameLangton on Holm. He was but of fve and twenty wínters, a faírsfaced man, yellowshaired,tall and strong; rather wiser than foolisher than young men are mostly wont; a valiant youth,& a kind; not of many words but courteous of speech;noroisterer,noughtmasterful, but peaceable and knowing how to fors bear: ín a fray a perílous foe, & a trusty warsfellow. His father, with whom he was dwelling when this tale begins,was a great merchant, richer than a baron of the land, a headsman of the greatest of the Líneages of Langton, and a captaín of the Porte; he was of the Líneage of the Goldings, therefore was he called 12 The first text page (reduced) of The Wood beyond the World (A27). The chapter title is in red. ‘The borders in this book, as well as the ten half-borders, are here used for the first time' (‘List’). The quarter sheet d2.7 (pp. 35–6, 45–6) had to be reprinted because the phrase (on p. 46) ‘somewhat uneasy going' was mistakenly printed as‘somewhat easy going'(see e below). Burne-Jones was paid f2o for designing the frontispiece; the total cost of production was £33o 18s. 5d. (Ledger). By the date of publication – which Cockerell gives in a letter (to Miss E. M. Evans, 19 October 1894 [LB]) as 18 rather than 16 October – all but a few copies had been sold. On 21 February 1895 Morris arranged for a cheap edition to be issued by Lawrence & Bullen: his royalties from that edition in 1895 were £69 (Cockerell diary, 21 February 1895 and 20 January 1896). COLLATION: 80: [a]10 b-r8. 138 leaves, pp. [2] [i] ii-xvi [2] [=20] [ı] 2-256. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 207 × 140 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm. TYPE: Golden type. NOTEs: On 8 March 1894 Bernard Quaritch wrote to Morris: Would you allow me to print at the Kelmscott Press a small volume: The Book of Wisdom and Lies[,] a Georgian storybook translated by Wardrop[.] The edition to be 25o, and you to undertake the supervision (not the proof-reading) to supply paper, reproduce a portrait etc. I have the translators Manuscript'(HEH HM 36666). Two days later Quaritch added: ‘I have sent the Ms. of the Georgian Tales to your address by parcel post. There is no necessity of giving a portrait and the Arms of Georgia. If you accept the printing of the Ms. it shall be with the understanding that neither I nor the translator interfere with you'(HEH HM 36667). On 17 March Morris sent Quaritch three sets of two specimen pages (see Dr.6) with this note: ‘I shall be glad to print it for you, and will do 25o copies at £,1.5.o per copy delivered to you bound in wrapping vellum with silk strings in the manner of my Kelmscott Press books'(BQ). Quaritch was pleased with the specimen pages and accepted Morris's terms on 19 March. I think the volume will form an original and respectable link in the series of Kelmscott press publications,’he declared (HEH HM 36668). Morris completed the design for the engraved title on 22 August 1894 (The Letters of William Morris to His Family and Friends, ed. Philip Henderson [195o], p. 36o). He purchased ten copies of the book for his own use (Cockerell to Quaritch, 1o October 1894 [LB]). Cost of production was £277 2s. 8d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Specimen pages (BQ). NOTEs: Ellis wrote to Morris on 6 January 1894: I quite agree with you as to the doubt whether so big a book as Shelley ought to make. 'I esteem all his work good except his juvenilia (written before Q.[ueen] Mab.). To make a volume of his shorter poems & lyrics would seem to me mean & unfair[.] But I doubt extremely if 3 fattish volumes would pay & I think perhaps therefore it is better to let him alone'(BL Add. Ms. 52715, f. 11). Ellis promised to consult Swinburne about the matter. On 17 January Cockerell recorded the decision in his diary: ‘w.M. is going to print a Shelley.' The usual silk ties were not used in the Shelley. The ties were omitted from the Shelley as an experiment, as some people don't care for them,’Cockerell wrote to Stopford Brooke on 14 December 1894, ‘but except in the 2 other volumes of this work, they will appear in all future books that are bound in vellum'(LB). In a letter to the publishers Dodd, Mead & Co., 29 March 1895, Cockerell said, I have had no other complaints of the binding of the Shelley, though, as the volumes are thick, I understand the objection. It is of course necessary to bind the 3 vols. uniformly' (LB). The vellum copies, however, do have ties. ‘Some of the half-borders designed for The Wood beyond the World [A27] reappear before the longer poems' (‘List’). Cost of production for the three volumes was £832 4s. (Ledger). COLOPHON: THUS ends this rhymed version of the / Penitential Psalms [ornament 1] Found in a Manu-/script of Horæ Beatæ Mariæ Virginis, writ-/ten at Gloucester about the year 144o, and / now transcribed and edited by F. S. Ellis. / Printed by William Morris at the Kelms-/cott Press, 14, Upper Mall, Hammersmith. / Finished on the 15th day of November, 1894. / [printer's mark 1] COLLATION: $8 ^ { \mathrm { o } } : [ { \mathrm { a } } ] ^ { 4 } \ { \mathrm { b } } - { \mathrm { e } } ^ { 8 } .$ 36 leaves, pp. [8] [1] 2-63 [64]. PAPER & DIMENS1ONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 207 × 140 mm. Text: 26 lines (including blank lines) × 88 mm. TYPE: Chaucer type. ORNAMENTs: Three-quarter borders; three- and six-line initials; Latin texts in red. CoNTENs: [a]ı-[a]4 blank [a]4 title br-e6r text e6 colophon $\mathrm { e } 7 ^ { \mathrm { r } } { - } \mathrm { e } 8 ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ glossary e8v blank. BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). Text of title-page printed in black on front cover. PUBLCATION: 300 paper copies (7s. 6d.); 12 vellum copies (3 gns.). Issued 10 December 1894. NOTEs: . . . the Rev. Professor Skeat has pointed out that the scribe must have copied them from an older manuscript, as they are in the Kentish dialect of about a century earlier. The half-border on p. 34 appears for the first time in this book' (List’). Ellis made considerable alterations in the text and consulted Morris and F. J. Furnivall while editing the volume (see b below). As usual, he and Morris disagreed about the editorial apparatus. On 2 November 1894 Morris wrote to him: ‘Tis clear that I dont take the same view of a glossary as you do; I dont think we want a series of synonyms, but in each case the exact modern equivalent of the word, and the new word should be the nearest possible, e.g. not “rigid" for stef", but stiff. In short all we want is some ½ dozen words like ord (Iclandic oddi) point, or thorns. Otherwise we have something which a reader of old English doesn't want, and which is not enough for a non-reader, who would want every word respelt or p[a]raphrased. Please excuse me; but you told me to knock the glossary about; now I have done so' (PML). Cost of production was £,71 13s. (Ledger). By 4 October 1894 the entire edition was subscribed for (Cockerell to Messrs. Cornish Bros. [LB]); hence it was not included in the κP list of ι December 1894 (C28). The book evoked one of Mrs. Morris's rare comments (in a letter to Blunt, 15 December [1894]): ‘Some small books are being printed at [the} same time [as the Chaucer], one is quite lovely, the penitential psalms in mediaeval verse - with a great deal of red – I fear it is all sold, those small things go off at once before one knows about them often'(FM[1]). Smith, 24 September 1895 (C). ## A3ī Girolamo Savonarola, Epistola de Contemptu Mundi Epistola de Contemptu Mundi di Frate / Hieronymo da Ferrara dellordine de frati / predicatori la quale manda ad Elena Buon-/accorsi sua madre, per consolarla della / morte del fratello, suo Zio. / [woodengraving] COLOPHON: [X7] [paragraph symbol] Questa edizione e la prima cavata / dall’autografo, da lungo tempo conser-/vato dalle monache del Malgio a Fir-/renze. Seque parole per parola l’origi-/nale & l’ortografia dell’autore, ma / e stampata senza le abbreviazioni, / perche lo stampatore non possiede i/ segni che sarebbero necessari. / [paragraph symbol] Le parole stampate in rosso, sono / logore o mancanti nell’originale, cheha / subito i guasti del tempo & della / umidita, & sono state riportate duana / copia antica della lettera essistente a / Firenze, pubblicata dal Padre Vincen-/zo Marchesi nel 18so. / [paragraph symbol] La figura del Frate sulla prima pa-/gina e stata disegnata dalle’editore Car/lo Fairfax Murray & incisa in legno da / Guglielmo H. Hooper. Tutti gli ornati / sono stati disegnati da Guglielmo / Morris. / [paragraph symbol] Di questa edizione privata fatta a / spese delle’editore, sono state tirate / cento cinquanta copie in carta, & sei in / pergamena. / [χ8] [paragraph symbol] Impresso in London per Guglielmo / Morris alla Stamperia Kelmscott./ Adi ultimo di novembre Mdccclxxxxiv. / [printer's mark 1] COLLATION: 80: X8. 8 leaves, PP. [1-3] 4-15 [16]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 207 × I40 mm. Text: 27 lines ×79 mm. TYPE: Chaucer type. ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATION: Border I; six-line initial; conjecturally restored words and printer's mark in red; wood-engraved illustration designed by Charles Fairfax Murray on title-page. CONTENTS: χI title χI blank χ2r-X7 text X7v- χ8 colophon χ8v blank. BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). No title on front cover. PUBLICATION: Iso paper copies; 6 vellum copies. Not for sale. Ready 12 December 1894. NOTEs: Charles Fairfax Murray began working for Morris & Co. in the 187os as an artist and copyist (Introductions, i, 26o); for many years he maintained a close relationship with Morris, Ruskin, Rossetti, and Burne-Jones, and in the latter part of his life he became celebrated as a connoisseur and collector. (See Carole Cable, Charles Fairfax Murray, Assistant to Dante Gabriel Rossetti', Library Chronicle of the University of Texas, NS 10 [1978], 81–9.) 'The little Psalmi Penitentiales,’said Morris, has not only an archaeological but a very high literary value' (Ideal Book, p. 1os). Since the text is in Italian, there is an unusually large number of typographical errors in the proofs (see d below). Cost of production was £,8 4s. 7d. (Ledger). On 28 December [1894] Ellis wrote to Murray, By no means the smallest value of the book is your lovely design on the title. If I do not entirely give credence to demoniacal possession I shall hereafter be a believer in angelical possession for the spirit of Fra Angelico or one of his friends must have taken possession of your fingers when you drew that exquisite design, instinct with the feeling of the 1sth century' (PML). Murray's wood-engraving was a close adaption of a woodcut in Epistola di Frate Hieronymo da Ferrara dellordine de frati predicatori a uno Amico (Florence, n.d. [late sixteenth century]). Murray also drew a few details from another woodcut portrait of Savonarola at his writing desk which appears, with slight variations, in a number of works by Savonarola that were printed in Florence and Venice in the early sixteenth century. The book was privately printed for Murray and was of course not for sale at the KP. Hence it is probably the only kP book that was ever inscribed to Morris (see k below). COLLATION: $4 ^ { \mathrm { { o } } } \colon [ \mathrm { a } ] ^ { 4 } ~ [ \mathrm { b } ] ^ { 8 } ~ \mathrm { c } - \mathrm { h } ^ { 8 } ~ \mathrm { i } ^ { 4 }$ 64 leaves, pp. [i-ii] iv-vi [2] [=8] [1] 2-119 [120]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Perch paper. Leaf: 290 × 2IC mm. Text: 32 lines × 130 mm (prelims and end matter) or 140 mm (text). TYPE: Text in Troy type; shoulder and side notes, argument, list of persons and places, and glossary in Chaucer type. CoNTENTs: [a]1-[a]1ν blank [a]2r title [a]2ν-[a]3 argument [a]4r blank [a]4 engraved title [b]1r-h7 text h8r colophon h8v-i3r list of persons and places i3v-i4 glossary i4 blank. NOTEs: Morris said of Beowulf that it was ‘the first and the best poem of the English race, [with] no author but the people' (A WS, ii, 494). On 21 February 1893 Morris began a translation bāsed on a paraphrase by Alfred John Wyatt, the Anglo-Saxon scholar (List’). Four days later Morris recorded in his diary: Finished the first lot of Beo: about 70o lines. Wrote Wyatt.' The later history of the translation can be traced in Cockerell's diary. 1o April 1894: ‘w.M. finished the Beowulf.’11 April 1894: ‘Mr Wyatt at K.[elmscott] H.[ouse] to go over the Beowulf with W.M.'23 June 1894: A J Wyatt at K.H. with w.M. revising the Beowulf.’ 25 June 1894: Looking through proofs of the Beowulf ... all day.'1o December 1894: ‘w.M. wrote the argument for the Beowulf, which was finally passed for press by him & Wyatt.' 4 February 1895: Beowulf published.'(Observe that this differs from the official date of publication given in the ‘List’.) ‘The borders in the book were only used once again, in the Jason [A34]'(‘List’). Cost of production was £485 4s. 4d. (Ledger). Morris claimed that he lost money on the book: It was announced in the prospectus that it would be published at two guineas but in the printing of the book several sheets got spoiled and had to be reprintêd. The mere cost of reprinting these sheets and the expense of the extra paper coverted my profit into a loss – and the book is sold at less than what it cost me to produce it’ (Ideal Book, p. 111). The translation by Morris and Wyatt was published by Longmans in August 1898. RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Preliminary drawing and design for engraved title (PML 76921). According to May Morris, ‘When finished the tale turned out to be so much longer than intended for a small book that, to meet the additional expense of production, the issue was increased to more than double the usual number. But for an edition of six hundred my father thought the price (fifteen shillings) so high that, to add to the attraction, he gave notice that he undertook not to reprint it in any cheaper edition' (Introductions, ii, soo-1). ENGRAVED TITLE: HAND & / SOUL / BY / DANTE / GA-BRIEL / ROSSETTI [roman lettering designed by Morris] COLOPHON (English edition): [ornament 2] HERE ends Hand and Soul, / written by Dante Gabriel Rossetti, / and reprinted from The Germ for / Messrs. Way and Williams of/ Chicago, by William Morris, at / the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, / Hammersmith [ornament 1] Finished the / 24th day of October, 1895. / [paragraph] [ornament 2] Sold by William Morris at / the Kelmscott Press. COLOPHON (American edition): [ornament 2] HERE ends Hand and Soul, / written by Dante Gabriel Rossetti, / and reprinted from The Germ by / William Morris, at the Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammer-/smith, England [ornament 1] Finished the / 24th day of October, 1895. / [paragraph] [ornament 1] Sold by Way and Williams, / Chicago. COLLATION: 160: [a]4 b-d8 e4. 33 leaves, pP. [8] [1] 2-56 [2] [=58]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 142× 102 mm. Text: 19 lines × 63 mm. TYPE: Golden type. ORNAMENTs: Borders 15a and 15; four- and six-line initials; epigraph, title on bı, shoulder notes, footnote on er, and first paragraph of colophon in red. coNTENTs: [a]ı-[a]2 blank [a]3 title [a]3 epigraph [a]4 blank [a]4 engraved title b1-e4 text e4 colophon. BINDING: Stiff vellum. Stamped in gold on spine: ‘HAND AND SOUL’ [reduced Golden type]. PUBLICATION: In England, 225 paper copies (1os.); 10 vellum copies (3os.). In America, 3oo paper copies (price not known); I vellum copies (\$3.50). Issued 12 December 1895. NOTEs: Rossetti wrote this story in December 1849 for the first number of the Germ. It was set in type for the 1869 edition of Rossetti's Poems, but the author decided not to include it and instead had a few copies printed for private distribution to his friends. The text was reprinted with minor alterations in the Fortnightly Review, 8. (December 1870), 692–702. On 29 April 1895 W. Irving Way, the Chicago publisher, called on Sydney Cockerell at the KP: . . . I ventured to remark that a new departure in a business way had made a trip to England necessary in order to establish some business connections there. My first thought was that Mr. Morris would consent to print a book for us, something that could be issued at a modest price. Now that “Child Christopher" was off the press, and “Chaucer" was progressing satisfactorily, there was a short interim in which Mr. Cockerell thought something might be undertaken, and indeed Mr. Morris himself had been considering a reprint of Rossetti's “Hand and Soul" among the possibilities.' Cockerell took Way over to Kelmscott House to meet Morris, who agreed to the joint venture. (Way, A Visit to William Morris', Modern Art [Boston], 4 [Summer, 1896], 79–80.) On 26 June 1895 Cockerell sent Way & Williams an estimate (see E7): the kP would charge five shillings per paper copy and fifteen shillings per vellum copy. In a second letter, dated 19 July (see E7), Cockerell explained that the prices quoted did not include carriage to the usA. ‘On the copies sold through our agency in London, Mr. Morris would expect a discount of 25% to cover discount to trade, printing of announcement etc., thus leaving you a profit of 2/6 on each paper copy sold. . . .' Printing began on 12 September 1895 (Cockerell diary), and on 14 December the American copies were shipped on the s.s. Untria (Cockerell to U.s. Express Company, 17 December 1895 [LB]). Cockerell wrote to Way & Williams on 17 December, 52o paper copies were printed, leaving 19o for us to sell after sending 5 to the Libraries, & deducting 25 for Mr. Morris' use. . . . I am sorry that we only had 6 vellum copies left when your letter asking for 12 reached us. . . . we find that the price you are paying us for the books will only just cover the expenses (if it does that), the cost of binding having been greater than was anticipated'(LB). In January 1896 Morris had twenty more copies bound up from overs, of which he sent eight to America.‘This makes the total number bound 4o i.e., 15 more than the number announced,’ Cockerell said in a letter to Way & Williams on 14 January, But there are always a few overs, & this is not an extravagant number proportion’ (LB). Way & Williams proposed another title, but Morris declined, being full of plans of his own'(Cockerell to Way & Williams, 2 February 1896 [LB]). Cost of production was £1o5 3s. 9d. (Ledger). Hand and Soul was the only kP book published in America and the only 16º book printed on vellum. RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Corrected (by Morris and Cockerell) proofs (Cockerell sale, no. 69; now Asu). (b) Inc. proofs (Saks sale, no. 157). (c) ‘Sheet e of both issues, showing the different colophons' (Cockerell's note) [PML ‘Morrisiana I’). (d) Cockerell's copy, with design for one initial and ‘I7 leaves (“overs") PRINTED ON VELLUM, showing the two different colophons'(Cockerell sale, no. 39; Blackwell cat. 65s, no. 384). (e) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 15 December 1895 (Dc). (f) Pres. copy to Theodore Watts, 15 January 1896 (mC). (g) Pres. copy to A. C. Swinburne, 15 January 1896 (Parke-Bernet cat., I1 January 1944, no. 536). (h) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 15 December 1895 (mc). (i) Pres. copy to Philip Webb, 19 December 1895 (τc). (j) Pres. copy to Jenny Morris, 22 December 1895 (FM[1]). (k) Copy with Morris's bookplate (uv). (l) Pres. copy from W. Irving Way to Mrs. Wynne, 29 January 1896 (BC). (m) Pres. copy to Harry Buxton Forman, 8 December 1895 (BC). ## A37 Poems Chosen out of the Works of Robert Herrick [ornament 2] POEMS CHOSEN OUT OF THE / WORKS OF ROBERT HERRICK. NOTEs: On 6 January 1894 Ellis wrote to Morris, ‘What do you think of Coleridge? his poems, excluding of course the dramas, would make a volume which would answer Mr. Wemmicks desideratum [possibly for it's a principle with me, if you have an idea, carry it out and keep it up'- Great Expectations, chap. xxv], and I should think there would be every chance of selling 25o or 3oo' (BL Add. Ms. 52715, f. 11). In an undated letter, printed by Mackail (ii, 325-6), Morris replied: As to the Coleridge-Keats question, you don't quite understand the position I think. Keats was a great poet who sometimes nodded: we don't want to make a selection of his works. Coleridge was a muddle-brained metaphysician, who by some strange freak of fortune turned out a few real poems amongst the dreary flood of inanity which was his wont. It is these real poems only that must be selected, or we burden the world with another useless book. Christabel only just comes in because the detail is fine; but nothing a hair's breadth worse must be admitted. There is absolutely no difficulty in choosing, because the difference between his poetry and his drivel is so striking. 'I have been through the poems, and find that the only ones that have any interest for me are – 1. Ancient Mariner, 2. Christabel, 3. Kubla Khan, 4. the poem called Love. This would make a very little book, about 6o pages. There is one other which at least has some character, though rather tainted with Wordsworthianism; it is called The Three Graves, and is about as cheerful as the influenza. But then it is copyright; and at the best it would rather water down the good ones.' The engraved title was sent to Keates for cutting on 17 December 1895 (see a below). Cost of production was £127 15s. 8d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Cockerell's copy, with design for engraved title (Cockerell sale, no. 41; now PML). (b) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 5 April 1896 (Parke-Bernet cat., 22 November 1955, no. 271). (c) Pres. copy (vellum) from Jane Morris to Cockerell, 2 September 1912, with a note by Cockerell (RC). (d) Pres. copy to Philip Webb, 13 April 1896 (τc). (e) Pres. copy to Jenny Morris, 2 April 1896 (FM[1]). NoTEs: The early history of The Well at the World's End – which was longer in production than any other title printed at the kP – is recorded in Cockerell's diary. 21 April 1892: ‘Saw . . . some proofs of the Well at the World's End.'15 September 1892: ‘Saw . . . trial pages of the Well at the World's End etc.'(One of these was set in a single column [List'].) 17 October 1982: ‘Morris busy on the 2nd of the four borders which he intends to have in“The Well at the World's End". . . . '19 October 1892: . . . read proofs of the first 2oo pages of the Well at the World's End – which is now at length approaching completion. Printing began on 16 December 1892 (‘List’). The book was announced in the κP list of December 1892 (C3) ‘with 4 woodcuts, designed by C. F. Murray'. In the spring of 1893 Morris decided to allow Arthur J. Gaskin to illustrate it (Cockerell diary, 23 April 1893). Writing to his daughter Jenny on 13 July 1893, Morris said, I found [here] 2 designs from Gaskin, small ones this time: I am sorry to say that I did not think they would quite do'(BL Add. Ms. 4534o, f. 122). The drawings were revised, and in the end Gaskin contributed nineteen designs (see Gaskin, pp. 44-7), some of which were engraved by Hooper in October and November 1893 (HEH HM 36655). A number of pages with Gaskin's illustrations were printed at the kP (see g, i, j, ν, w, x, γ, z, and aa below). On 2o February 1895, however, Morris sought the advice of Philip Webb, who agreed with Morris's unfavourable judgement of Gaskin's work; on 26 February Morris wrote to Gaskin declining to use his illustrations (Morris diary). Though Gaskin asked to be allowed to do four new illustrations (Morris diary, 27 February and I March 1895), Morris turned instead to Burne-Jones, whose designs were engraved as usual by Hooper (HE HM 36655). The text was set from the sheets of the Longmans edition, printed at the Chiswick Press, which was simultaneously in production (see b below). According to Sparling, . . . the last sheet [of the kP edition] . . . had to stand by until à press was available, two being fully ![](images/d058716fd3599a9e7548fe5a929beb8218a5dccfcf9419526a7a811fb890a181.jpg) 14 A rejected wood-engraving (reduced) designed by Arthur J. Gaskin for The Well at the World's End (A39). The design is in a private collection; proofs of this and other illustrations designed by Gaskin for The Well are scattered about in a number of libraries. None of them was used in the published book, which was instead illustrated by four engravings designed by Edward Burne-Jones. occupied upon the Chaucer [A4o] and a third upon the Earthly Paradise $\left[ { \tt A } _ { 4 } \mathrm { I } \right] ^ { \prime } \left( \mathrm { p } . \mathrm { \ 7 9 } \right)$ The eight borders and the six different ornaments between the columns, appear here for the first time, but are used again in The Water of the Wondrous Isles [A45], with the exception of two borders' (List’). NoTEs: Chaucer had been one of the great discoveries of Morris and Burne-Jones at Oxford in the 185os, and from the beginning Morris envisioned an edition of his works as one of the likely publications of the κP. Cockerell recorded in his diary on I June 1891 that Morris thinks of printing a Chaucer from a blackletter fount which he hopes to design'. In a proof (BL C.102.h.18) of the first list of the KP, May 1892, among the titles described as in preparation is THE POEMS OF GEOFFREY CHAUCER. Black Letter. Large 4to'. In the published version of the list (Cı) this title was deleted, but in the list of December 1892 (C3) the Chaucer was finally announced: ‘Chaucer's Works. With about 6o designs by E. Burne-Jones.’ Thereafter the letters and diaries of the period are filled with references to the progress (or lack thereof) of the Chaucer, which was recognised by all concerned, even before its publication, as the most ambitious and remarkable of the KP books. Before entering into a discussion of several specific aspects of its production, it may be useful to outline the chronology of the book. In January 1892, when the Troy type was delivered, Morris had two trial pages set, but it was immediately obvious that a smaller type was needed. Writing to Frances Jenkinson on 14 March 1892, Ellis commented: . . . I enclose you a specimen of the new type in which as you see Caxton's “Recuyel" is to be & Chaucer is to follow but in a size smaller type but same pattern – and in double cols. To print Chaucer in this size wd. be delightful but I fear buyers would turn resentful[?] at the size which the book would necessarily make'(cuL Add. 6463, no. 2o18). By July 1892 another trial page, in the newly cut Chaucer type, was printed and was adopted as the basis for the book: two columns of fifty-eight lines each. ‘The idea of the Chaucer as it now exists, with illustrations by Sir Edward Burne-Jones, then took definite shape'(List’). Mr. Morris began designing his first folio border on Feb. I, 1893, ![](images/1ef40de29b253cb091e3c454ba284ca3663100dc76017787f44c60c5be2ac727.jpg) ![](images/16cb0592d49669c7cf20d4e3d9f93639c214b4521f3b2ef9e392548c22eebdaf.jpg) AS OLDE SCORIES CELLEN US, Ther was a duc that highte Theseus; OfHtthenes he was lord and governour, And in his tyme swich a conquerour, That gretter was ther noon under the sonne. ful many ariche contree hadde he wonne; Chat with hís wysdom and his chivalrye He conquered al the regne of femenye, That whilom was ycleped Scithia; And weddede the queéne Ypolita, And broghte hire hoom with hym in his contree ith muchel glorie and greet solempnytee, And eek hir faire suster Emelye. And thus with victorie and with melodye Lete I this noble duc to Atthenes ryde, And al his hoost, in armes hym bisyde. And certes, if it nere to long to heere, I wolde have toold yow fully the manere, How wonnen was the regne of femenye By Cheseus, and by his chivalrye; And of the grete bataille for the nones Bitwixen Atthenes and Amazones; And how asseged was Ypolita, Che faire hardy queene of Scithia; And of the feste that was at hir weddynge, And of the tempest at hir hoom comynge; But al that thyng I moot as now forbere. I have, God woot, a large feeld to ere, And wayke been the oxen in my plough. ![](images/5241e0581f4de860e825b89e915fffc2be6a8be4d68c80aba1ac551859b2e9d7.jpg) 18 The Works of Geoffrey Chaucer (A4o), p. 9 (reduced). The design for the illustration (by Edward Burne-Jones) is in the Fitzwilliam Museum. A proof of an earlier state of the wood-engraving is in the Clark Library. but was dissatisfied with the design and did not finish it. Three days later he began the vine border for the first page, and finished it in about a week, together with the initial word "Whan,"the two lines of heading, and the frame for the first picture, and W. H. Hooper engraved the whole of these on one block. The first picture was engraved at about the same time'(‘List'). In an announcement of 14 November 1894 (C27) the number of illustrations was altered toupwards of 7o’and the number of copies to be printed was increased from 325 to 425 – the latter decision being a result of Morris's growing financial anxiety about the book. Printing of the Chaucer began on 8 August 1894 (Cockerell diary). At the beginning of the next year another press was set up in Sunderland Cottage, 2ı Upper Mall, to hasten the progress of the book. Burne-Jones finished the illustrations in December 1895. On 1o December 1895 Morris began designing the special pigskin binding (Cockerell diary), and on 19 February 1896 he started working on the engraved title (Morris diary). The first two copies of the book were deliveręd to Morris and Burne-Jones on 2 June 1896. Douglas Cockerell, on 24 June, brought to Morris in Folkestone the first pigskinbound copy. ‘Very satisfactory,’Morris noted in his diary. Morris's first task in planning the book was to find a suitable text for the Chaucer. Ellis, just before travelling to Cambridge, wrote to Frances Jenkinson on 17 April 1892, I shall also take the opportunity of calling on Mr. Skeat if he is in Cambridge to hear what he says as to the text of the projected Kelmscott Press folio Chaucer'(cuL Add. 6463, no. 2046). Walter W. Skeat, Professor of Anglo-Saxon at Cambridge University, was in fact at that moment preparing his monumental edition of Chaucer, which the Clarendon Press was to begin publishing in 1894, and it must have been immediately obvious to Ellis and Morris that this was the text they were searching for. But securing permission to reprint it from the Delegates of the Oxford University Press proved to be unusually difficult. When the Delegates first considered the request in November 1892, they gave a negative response (C. E. Doble to Skeat, 29 November 1892 [oUA: DUP/PUB/- I/s6]). Skeat suggested to Morris, as an alternative, that he might reprint the first edition of Chaucer (Doble to Skeat, 6 December 1892 [OUA: DUP/PUB/I/56]). For the time being Morris left the matter in abeyance, though Ellis continued to study Chaucer's text in preparation for the KP edition. At a meeting of the Delegates on 26 May 1894 the request was renewed, again without effect: according to the minutes, Letter read from the Revd. Prof. W. W. Skeat proposing that the Delegates should grant permission to Mr. Wm. Morris to reprint the Oxford text of“Chaucer"amongst his Kelmscott Editions on condition of a contribution being made by him to the proposed English Lectureship Funds at Cambridge. Resolved that the Delegates think it would be injurious to the Clarendon Press edition to accede to Mr. Morris' proposal.' On 9 August 1894 Morris sent to the Secretary of the Clarendon Press a letter of clarification: May I ask you to be so kind as to lay before the Delegates the request to allow me to make use of Professor Skeat's Chaucer under the following circumstances. I am publishing a Chaucer, which is being printed at the Kelmscott Press, & will be ornamented with picture[s] designed by Sir Edward Burne-Jones, and borders &c by myself. This book I hope to make a specially beautiful one as to typography & decoration, and I naturally wish to make the text as good as possible. I ask therefore to be allowed to avail myself of the corrections of errors which Professor Skeat's learning & acumen are making public, though we (Mr. F. S. Ellis, the editor, & myself) by no means intend to produce a literal reprint of his text. We should be pleased to make full acknowledgment of our obligations to Professor Skeat in the book itself. I should add that it is impossible that the book could come into competition with the text now being published at the University Press, as only 325 copies will be issued, at a high price (£,2o), & without notes or commentary. It is intended to be essentially a work of art' (OUA: DUP/PUB/1I/23). In the meantime, Morris had begun printing the Canterbury Tales, for which he used the text of the Ellesmere manuscript. The Delegates, at their meeting of 19 October 1894, at last gave Morris permission to reproduce ‘a limited number of readings from Prof. Skeat's Text of “Chaucer". . . , provided due acknowledgment of the sanction of the Editor and the Delegates be made'. In fact, as the marked copy of Skeat's edition at Yale (see a below) shows, Morris adopted the Skeat text almost verbatim with only a few minor emendations. The initials and names of Morris's printers in this copytext also reveal that the following compositors, in descending order of frequency, set the bulk of the Chaucer (excluding the Canterbury Tales): F. Collins, T. Binning, J. Tippett, Barks, H. Howes, and L. Tasker. Another major problem with which Morris had to wrestle while producing the Chaucer was his business relationship with Bernard Ouaritch, who had published three of the early kP books (A7, A8, and Aro) and had hoped to publish the Chaucer as well. Morris was annoyed when Quaritch raised the price of The Golden Legend on the day of publication (see NoTEs for A7) and eventually decided to publish future kp books himself; hence their negotiations over the Chaucer were often tense and suspicious in tone. The first serious misunderstanding arose in August 1894 when Quaritch in effect undersold Morris by discounting at a higher rate to dealers. ‘Since as I understand now, your circular has already been sent out, I am driven to offer the Chaucer to the trade at 2o per cent discount,'Morris wrote to Quaritch on 16 August 1894. Furthermore, Morris declined to supply him with‘any more copies, beyond the 63 already booked, at a higher discount than 15 per cent until all my trade customers have been supplied by me’(BQ). Quaritch grudgingly accepted these new terms, because he had no choice but to do so, but he complained to his son on 2o August, Mr. Wm Morris will call on me tomorrow; he is laying down the law to me about the Chaucer, – and I must submit. This has cooled my ardour to obtain the transfer of his publications from Reeves & Turner. Mr. Morris's success has turned him into a despot'(Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 194). The following day Morris ‘did not dare to show his face'but instead sent Cockerell, whom Quaritch found to be a very sharp fellow'. My friendship for Morris is at an end,’Quaritch lamented (Quaritch to B. Alfred Quaritch, 21 August 1894 [Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 195]). He agreed to buy sixty-three copies at a twenty-five per cent discount and fifty-three at a fifteen per cent discount. When Morris decided to print an additional hundred copies, Quaritch at once leaped forward with a proposal that he buy fifty of them – but at the original discount of twenty-five per cent (Quaritch to Morris, 2o November 1894 [HEH HM 36669]). Mr. W. Morris & I are on the road of being reconciled, he sees now how hastily he acted,'Quaritch wrote to his son on 12 November (Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 197). The quarrel with Quaritch was, in short, patched over in the end. Another key figure in the history of the Chaucer, but one with whom Morris almost never quarreled, was Edward Burne-Jones, the illustrator. Burne-Jones worked on the Chaucer designs only on Sundays, when Morris came to talk as he drew: on one such occasion, Burne-Jones boasted, we never got to speak of anyone later than the ninth century' (Burne-Jones to Frances Horner, n.d. (FM[1]). The elaborate procedure used to translate his designs into wood-engravings, employing the skill of Robert Catterson-Smith, has been described earlier in this bibliography (see Introduction, above). To one of his correspondents Burne-Jones explained his pleasure in the book as it progressed: And I have been calculating that . . . the time I have given to the Chaucer work in the last two years and a half is exactly to an hour the time I should have spent in visits from Saturday till Monday at “houses,"if I had been amiable and sociable. . . . And yet I know quite well not ten people in the land will care twopence about it. Yet it will be a very nice book – but it should have come out when Morris and I were at Óxford in our first term, and we should have lost our senses with bliss. . . . I have fourteen more designs to make and then my share is done, and I may permit myself the luxury of one smile. I have always been happy over it: it has never tired me but refreshed me always’(Burne-Jones to Frances Horner, 29 August 1895 (FM[1]). Burne-Jones's studio assistant, T. M. Rooke, records in his diary that on 2 March 1896 Morris, ‘very ghostlike, feeble & old looking’, appeared at Burne-Jones's door. Burne-Jones said that the Chaucer was the finest book ever printed – if w.M. had done nothing else it would be enough’. Morris replied: I must own I'm complacent about it – must try though not to be too conceited. And there's one thing that's not to be forgotten, that you backed me up well in it, old chap. If you'd been at all slack over it and hadn't been as much excited about it as I was, we should never have got through with it.' Besides the eighty-seven woodcut illustrations designed by Sir Edward Burne-Jones and engraved by W. H. Hooper, the Chaucer contains a woodcut title, fourteen large borders, eighteen different frames round the illustrations, and twenty-six initial words designed for the book by William Morris. Many of these were engraved by C. E. Keates, and others by W. H. Hooper and W. Spielmeyer'(List’). An announcement issued on 26 February 1896 (C4o) indicated that Morris would design four special bindings for the Chaucer: a full and a half pigskin executed at the Doves Bindery, and a full and a half pigskin executed by Leighton. Cockerell states that only the Doves full pigskin design was comnpleted, and it of course was carried out on forty-eight copies; however, in Butler Library, Columbia University, there is a dummy Chaucer in a full pigskin binding executed by Leighton and evidently designed by Morris. The cost of production was £7217 11d. (Ledger). A discriminating and enthusiastic review in the Athenaeum (3 October 1896, pp. 444-5) began: ‘This great edition of Chaucer is certainly the crowning achievement of the Kelmscott Press. I [II] [I1I] [IV] [V] [VI] [VI] [VIII]’ [Golden type on vol. i; reduced Golden type on vols. ii-vi] PUBLICATION: 225 paper copies (3os. per volume); 6 vellum copies (7 gns. per volume). Vol. i issued 24 July ī896; vol. ii issued 17 September 1896; vol. iii issued 5 December 1896; vol. iv issued 22 January 1897; vol. v issued 9 March 1897; vol. vi issued 1ı May 1897; vol. vii issued 29 July 1897; vol. vi issued 27 September 1897. NOTEs: Morris designed the first border in June 1895.‘I have been working hard at a border all day; but it has not come out right,’ he wrote to Jenny on 18 June. . . . never mind about the border. You see I am trying something new & can't expect to succeed at first go off’ (BL Add. Ms. 45340, f. 176). None of the ten borders used in The Earthly Paradise appear in any other book. The four different half-borders round the poems to the months are also not used elsewhere'(List’). The Earthly Paradise was first published in 1868–7o; Morris during the 186os laid plans, never realised, for an edition to be elaborately illustrated by Burne-Jones (see Joseph R. Dunlap, The Book That Never Was [New York, 1971] and A. R. Dufty's introduction to Morris's The Story of Cupid and Psyche [1974]). A single-volume, revised edition was issued by Reeves & Turner in 189o. For a discussion of the alterations in the KP edition, see Introductions, i, 1o5. This was the first KP book in which the Apple paper was used. ‘The size,’ according to the list of ı June 1896 (C44), ‘will be a new one, intermediate between the large 4to and the 8vo books hitherto issued.' To an interviewer in 1895 Morris commented: I am rather exercised as to its format but Cockerell is in favour of this new size – a sort of mild quarto, and yet looking like an octavo' (Ideal Book, p. 112). Cost of production was £855 18s. 7d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALs: (a) Preliminary drawings by Burne-Jones, possibly for illustrations (v & A[2] PD 52b). (b) Cockerell's copy, with designs for engraved title, initial, and ornament, and cancelled colophon of vol. iv; the following note by Cockerell appears in vol. i: This copy consists of the sheets that were brought in from the Press for William Morris to inspect as each one was finished. Only three volumes of the Earthly Paradise were finished in his lifetime and after his death the sheets were similarly submitted to me. sCc'(Cockerell sale, no. 47; Blackwell cat. 6ss, no. 382). (c) Designs for two borders, one rejected (cL). (d) Design for a border (Sotheby cat., 26 July 1951, no. 553). (e) Design for border on a proof of April' (Sotheby cat., 7 July 1919, no. 241). (f) Proof of border around engraved title withoriginal design for line ending by William Morris' (Cockerell's note). (cL). (g) Design for border, with note by Morris: Keates / Sept '95' (mc). (h) Designs for two three-quarter borders, one rejected (cL). (i) ‘These proofs of the twelve month-poems . . . were made with a view to special initials being designed for them by William Morris. The border round the poem for March was drawn by him but he was not satisfied with it & it was not engraved'(Cockerell's note) (Cockerell sale, no. 72; now Yale Ip. M834 868Dma). (j) Overlay used in printing the first page of ‘Atalanta's Race', vol. i (HEH Morrisiana 12’). (k) Design for border on proof of March'(v & A[2] 1.128). (l) Proofs of March'with preliminary drawings of initial and borders (HEH Morrisiana 12’). (m) Two proofs of March', one with a preliminary drawing for a corner border (Specimens', no. 176). (n) Trial pages, first page of marked copytext, and Morris's estimate of length and price of book (HEH Morrisiana 12’). (o) Trial pages (BL C.43.c.26 [ΣFF2o]). (p) Inc. corrected (by Morris) proofs (BL C.43.f.24 [∆b14]). (q) Proofs of two pages (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], nos. 196–7). (r) Cancelled colophon of vol. iv (BL C.43.c.26 [ΣFF2o]). (s) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 24 August 1896 (in vol. i) (Sotheby cat., 7 July 1919, no. 224). (t) Philip Webb's copy (τC). (u) Jenny Morris's copy (Fm[1]). (γ) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 24 August 1896 (Sotheby cat., 22 December 1952, no. 78). ## A42 Laudes Beatæ Mariæ Virginis ## LAUDES BEATAE MARIAE VIRGINIS. INSERTED NOTE (145 × 210 mm, Chaucer type; ‘Specimens', no. 128: 3oo. Perch'; distributed to subscribers after the book's publication and in most copies of the book): LAUDES BEATAE MARIAE VIRGINIS. / The Reverend E. S. Dewick has pointed out that these poems / were printed in 1579, in a 16mo volume, with the title Psalterium / Divæ Virginis Mariæ, rhythmice conscriptum a Reverendissimo / domino Stephano, olim Archiepiscopo Cantuariensi, ex Biblio-/theca Tegrinseensi depromptum, Cantandum sub melodia, Pa-/tris sapientia. Cum gratia & privilegio Cæsareo. Excusum Te-/gernsee. Anno MDLXXIX. This Tegernsee edition contains a / Conclusio of four verses in the same metre as the Aves, but the / text is otherwise inferior to that printed by William Morris. The / ascription of the authorship to Stephen Langton is doubly in-/teresting, as the manuscript transcribed for the Kelmscott Press / was probably written before his death in 1288. / Dec. 28th, 1896. COLLATION: Large 40: [a]4 b-c8 d4. 24 leaves, PP. [8] [1] 2-34 [6] [=40]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Perch paper. Leaf: 290 × 210 mm. Text: 30 lines (6 poems of 5 lines each, with half-line spaces between poems) × 139 mm. TYPE: Troy type. ORNAMENTs: Half borders; six- and eight-line initials; texts of Psalms in red; alternate initials in blue. coNTENTs: [a]ı-[a]3 blank [a]4 title [a]4ν blank bı-dr text dıν colophon d2r-d4v blank. BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). Text of title-page printed in black on front cover. PUBLICATION: 25o paper copies (ios.); 10 vellum copies (2 gns.). Issued 7 August 1896. NOTEs: Morris purchased an early thirteenth-century English psalter, which he called the Nottingham Psalter, on 25 May 1893 for £115 (WMA B, p. 3s). One section of the Psalter, a Laudes beatae Mariae virginis, which Morris printed, was attributed either to Stephen Langton or to John Peckham, Archbishop of Canterbury. Other manuscripts of the Laudes exist. In the Nottingham Psalter version, the opening words of each psalm are in red, followed by a quatrain which applies them to the Virgin. (For a description of the Psalter, which is now in the Morgan Library, see the Morgan Catalogue, iv, 37–40.) This was the first kP book printed in three colours. The trial pages (see b, c, and d below) reveal more than usual variation: two, for example, are set in the Golden and Chaucer types, and several are in black and red only. Cost of production was £86 11s. I1d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) ‘Trials of blue ink for B.V.M.'(Cockerell's note) (HE ‘Morrisiana 12’). (b) Trial pages (BL C.43.f.24 [∆b23] and [∆b12]). Morris did not originally intend to include The Cuckow and the Nightingale; in a letter to Ellis, 8 April 1896, he wrote, ‘You see I don't want to print anything but the Flour & the Leaf; I don't see any necessity for making it a thick book; & I think it would spoil the poem to couple it up with another. However let us wait & see how the F & L pans out when it is printed'(FM[1]: Ellis autograph album, f. 30). The two initial words were used earlier in the Chaucer (A4o). The first bound copy of the book arrived at Kelmscott House an hour or two before Morris's death (Cockerell to Robert Proctor, 9 November 1896 [BL Add. Ms. 52743, f. 238]). Wilfrid Scawen Blunt also mentions this circumstance, adding that Morris had never read The Cuckow and the Nightingale ‘and was waiting to do so for it to be in print' (Blunt diary, 6 June 1898). BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: THE WATER / OF THE / WONDROUS / ISLES / BY / WILLIAM / MORRIS' [Golden type]. NOTEs: Morris ‘began [his] new romance in verse’on 4 February 1895, but on 8 February after seeing B[urne]-J[ones] w.M. decided to write the new romance in prose & verse, & began to rewrite it' (Cockerell diary). Eventually Morris wrote it in prose alone. (The verse fragment is in BL Add. MSs. 45322-3, 45325.) It was first announced as in the press in the list of June 1, 1896 [see C44], at which date the early chapters were in type, although they were not printed until about a month later'(‘List’). In Cockerell's diary we catch glimpses of the dying Morris attempting in vain to complete the design of the initial word ‘Empty'. 4 September 1896: ‘He sat up & was going to design “Empty"for the 3rd part of The Water of the Wondrous Isles – but only just managed to black in the spaces, & just rough in the word.'6 September 1896: w.M. rather tired, but decidedly no worse. He had done a little more to “Empty".'But, as the colophon indicates, Catterson-Smith had to finish the design. May Morris believed that her father had seen only the first two parts of the book through proofs (Introductions, ii, 575), though Cockerell was convinced that Morris had read more proofs than that (Cockerell to May Morris, 14 September 1912 [BL Add. Ms. 52740, f. 12]). May helped in proofing the rest of the book (Cockerell to May Morris, 19 January 1913 [BL Add. Ms. 52740, f. 14]). Cost of production was £158 1os. 3d. (Ledger). A less expensive edition was published by Longmans in October 1897. RELATED MATERIALS: (a) MS of text (BL Add. MSS. 45322-3). (m) Copy (vellum) with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (PCL). ## A46 Froissart's Chronicles (specimen pages) No title-page. COLOPHoN: [ornament 1] These two trial pages of the projected / edition of Lord Berners' translation of / Froissart were printed at the Kelmscott / Press in September, 1897, to preserve the / designs made for the work by William Morris [ornament 1] In the border are the arms of / France, the Empire, and England; on the / second page are those of Reginald Lord / Cobham, Sir Walter Manny, and Sir John / Chandos. COLLATION: 20: [a]2. 2 leaves, PP. [4]. VELLUM & DIMENSIONS: Vellum. Leaf: 424×283 mm. Text: two columns of 63 lines × 87 mm ([a]1v) or 94 mm ([a]2r) each. TYPE: Text in Chaucer type; title of Prologue in Troy type. ORNAMENTs: Border 32 (which includes a twenty-one-line initial word); half border; three- and ten-line initials; chapter titles and shoulder notes in red. CONTENTS: [a] colophon [a]1v-[a]2r text [a]2ν blank. BINDING: Not bound. PUBLICATION: No paper copies; 16o vellum copies (I gn.). Issued 7 October 1897. NOTEs: In 1895 Morris described his projected Froissart in these words to an interviewer: ‘You cannot have a better text than old Berners's. It's fine old English and would take a lot of beating. The edition of the text I am using is the two volumes quarto published by Rivington and others in 1812. My reprint is a full folio and will take up two volumes. I also intend to publish it in four parts. Those who may like a strong binding will be able to get one from me for both the Chaucer [A40] as well as the Froissart. It will be in white pigskin – a beautiful mațerial for wearing and showing designs – and the designs will be by Cobden-Sanderson. . . . no book that I could do would give me half the pleasure I am getting from the Froissart. I am simply revelling in it. It's such a noble and glorious work, and every page as it leaves the press delights me more than I can say. I am taking great pains with it, and doing all I can to realise what I have long wished (Ideal Book, Pp. 111−12). Berners' translation of Froissart (Morris's copy is now at kM) had been a favourite of Morris's since his college days. As soon as his Troy type was delivered from the foundry, he had a single-column trial page set up in January 1892 (see b below); early in 1893 double-column trial pages in the Chaucer type were produced (List’). In March of that year Morris was showing initials esigned for the Froissart to his friends (Cockerell diary, 1 March 1893). On 8 October 1893 Cockerell noted in his diary: ‘E[dward] B[urne]-J[ones] had that morning offered to do a big St. George & some shields of arms for the Froissart, which will put the book on the shelf for a while. Jason to be printed instead.' The subject of the frontispiece of volume i was to be St. George, that of volume ii Fame; it was understood that Emery Walker would do the heraldic research (Introductions, ii, 43s). Sparling was to be the editor (Sparling, p. 11). Though it was several times announced as in the press, the Froissart continued to be held in abeyance while work on the Chaucer progressed. It was definitely abandoned only after Morris's death. In November 1896‘about thirty-four pages were in type, but no sheet had been printed'(‘List’). During the following month thirty-two copies of sixteen of those pages (see Di.8) were privately distributed to friends of Morris. ‘This suggested the idea of printing two pages for wider distribution. The half-border had been engraved in April, 1894, by W. Spielmeyer, but the large border only existed as a drawing. It was engraved with great skill and spirit by C. E. Keates, and the two pages were printed by Stephen Mowlem, with the help of an apprentice, in a manner worthy of the designs' (List’). According to a note by Cockerell, only about a half-dozen (perhaps not so many) copies'were printed with the colophon on the first rather than the last page, as I came to the conclusion it was a mistake' (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], no. 202). This statement seems to be one of Cockerell's rare inaccuracies, because on all the copies I have examined the colophon appears on the first page. A facsimile of these specimen pages was produced by Frederick Goudy, the American type-designer, in 1918. When Swinburne received his copy of the sixteen pages, he wrote to Cockerell, 2o January 1897, ‘A thousand thanks for your beautiful & precious gift. Why did not Morris take up & finish Froissart earlier – instead of the less appropriate work (Shelley, for one instance) on which the Kelmscott Press was occasionally wasted'(PML MA 3000 R-V). Cost of production, including all expenses incurred for the abandoned Froissart, was £108 5s. 7d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Designs for eight initials (CL ‘red portfolio'). (b) Trial pages, some with preliminary drawings of coats of arms, and inc. proofs (BC). (c) Trial pages, designs for ornaments and initials, and a ‘list in Morris's autograph of the Coats-of-arms that would be needed, with notes by Sir Emery Walker' (Cockerell sale, no. 76). (d) Inc. proofs (su). (e) Inc. corrected proofs (Saks sale, no. 174). (f) Philip Webb's copy (τC). (g) Copy with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (Perry collection). ## A47 Sire Degrevaunt ## SIRE DEGREVAUNT COLOPHON: [ornament 2] Edited by F. S. Ellis after the edition / printed by J. . Halliwell from the Cam-/bridge MS., with some additions & varia-/tions from that in the Library of Lincoln / Cathedral. Printed by William Morris at / the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Ham-/mersmith, in the County of Middlesex, / and finished on the 14th day of March, 1896. / [printer's mark 1] / Sold by William Morris at the Kelmscott / Press. COLLATION: 80; [a]⁴ b–f⁸ g4. 48 leaves, pP. [8] [1] 2–81 [82] [6] [=88]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 207×I43 mm. Text: 25 lines (including blank lines) × 88 mm. TYPE: Chaucer type. ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATION: Borders Ia and I; three- and ten-line initials; title on bı, explicit, and shoulder notes in red; wood-engraved frontispiece designed by Burne-Jones. CONTENTS: $ { \left[ { \mathrm { a } } \right] } _ { 1 } \mathrm { r } - { \left[ { \mathrm { a } } \right] } _ { 2 } \mathrm { v }$ blank [a]3 title $[ \mathfrak { a } ] _ { 3 } \mathfrak { v } _ { - } [ \mathfrak { a } ] _ { 4 } \mathfrak { r }$ blank [a]4ν frontispiece bı-gı text g1 colophon $\mathrm { g 2 ^ { r } \mathrm { - } g 4 ^ { v } }$ blank. BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). Printed in black on front cover: ‘SIRE DEGREVÀÛNT [two ornaments 3]’ [Chaucer type]. PUBLICATION: 350 paper copies (I5s.); 8 vellum copies (4 gns.). Issued 12 November 1897. NOTEs: The text is based upon the Camden Society's Thornton Romances (see NOTEs for A33), in which James Halliwell wrote that ‘Sir Degrevant . . . is, perhaps, more curious and valuable than the [other romances in the volume]. The descriptive notices of early costume and architecture, are of peculiar interest; and it would perhaps be difficult to select a romance of the kind of more merit'(p. xxii). The story had long been a favourite in Morris's circle: in the summer of 186o Burne-Jones did a wall-painting of The Wedding Procession of Sire Degrevaunt in the drawing room of Red House, Morris's home. The text follows closely the Halliwell version except that brackets around conjectural readings are omitted. A more recent editor of the romance has described the kP edition as ‘a pleasant book to read, but useless for textual study' (L. F. Casson, ed., The Romance of Sir Degrevant [Early English Text Society, 1949], p. xv). The frontispiece was not printed until October, 1897, eighteen months after the text was finished' (List’). On 18 October 1897 Cockerell wrote to Leighton, ‘Please send for the sheets of Syr Degrevaunt & the blue papers for the covers, on Friday. You will then be able to go ahead & to issue Syr Isambras [A48] & Syr Degrevaunt together at the end of the month'(LB). A fimsy was inserted by Leighton between the frontispiece and the first page of the text, the only instance in which this was done in any κP book. Cost of production was £48 3s. 7d. (Ledger). (e) Corrected proofs (Bod. Kelmscott Press e.- 27\*; described in Bodleian Library Record, 8 [February 1969], 163). (f) Revises of pp. 7, 21, and 27 (BL C.43.c.26 [ΣFF2o]). (g) Cockerell's copy (Cockerell sale, no. 52). (h) Philip Webb's copy (τc). (i) C. H. (k) Burne-Jones's copy (Sotheby cat., 7 July 1919, no. 229). Cockerell (ed.), Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century ## SOME GERMAN WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH / CENTURY COLOPHON: [[e]6] Here ends Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century, for / which the blocks (with one exception) were prepared by Walker / and Boutall under the direction of the late William Morris. Now / edited by S. C. Cockerell, and printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper / Mall, Hammersmith. Finished on the 15th day of December, 1897. / [[e]6v] [printer's mark 2] / Sold by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press. FOREWORD: Of the 35 reproductions of German woodcuts here given, 29 are all / that were done of a series chosen by William Morris to appear in / the Catalogue of his library, which was to have been annotated by / him and printed at the Kelmscott Press. The other 6 were made for / his article in the 4th number of Bibliographica on the Early Wood-/cut Books of Ulm & Augsburg, and thanks are due to Messrs. Ke-/gan Paul, Trench, Trübner & Co. for the loan of the blocks, as well / as for permission to quote the portions of the article which serve as / an introduction to this book. These quotations will show the nature / of the remarks that Mr. Morris intended to write for the Catalogue, / of which they would have been the most interesting feature. A minor / feature was to have been a statement of the number of different cuts, / and the number of cuts including repetitions, in the principal illus-/trated books. For this purely scientific side of the work I was made / responsible, and as the early printers usually repeated their cuts, and / no one has hitherto troubled to count them in this way, it has seemed / worth while to preserve these few statistics now that the original de-/sign of the Catalogue has fallen through. They are accordingly print-/ed as an appendix in a list of the more important woodcut books that / the library contained. For some particulars in this section I am / indebted to my friend Mr. Robert Proctor, who has kindly looked / over the proofsheets, and pointed out errors and omissions. / [paragraph] I have been tempted to add a list of the manuscripts, which, though / comparatively few in number (112) were the most notable part of the / library; but without a full description of each volume such a list would / be meaningless, and anything more than a list would be out of place. / S. C. COCKERELL. Printed in black on front cover: ‘SOME ĠERMAN WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH CENTURY' [Golden type] PUBLICATION: 225 paper copies (3os.); 8 vellum copies (s gns.). Issued 6 January 1898. NOTEs: Morris asked Cockerell‘to help him to catalogue his library' on the evening of 19 October 1892 (Cockerell diary), and for the next few years Cockerell, in addition to his other duties, studied and catalogued Morris's extensive collection of incunabula and mediaeval manuscripts. Out of this activity grew Morris's scheme of a heavily illustrated catalogue of his library, with descriptions by Cockerell and additional notes by himself. To an interviewer in 1895, Morris said, ‘This will be largely illustrated with facsimiles of the plates and pages of the book themselves. Of course, this is just a little fancy for my own pleasure, and yet I have an idea that there ought to be many book-lovers and art-lovers who would find much in the volume to delight them. . . . You see that I am sparing no trouble to give the finest reproductions' (Ideal Book, p. 112). Two trial pages of the projected catalogue have survived (BL C.43.- f.24 [∆b12] and C.102.h.18). The first is a description of a psalter, N.E. France, c. 126o, on a leaf of Flower paper (281 × 204 mm), with a text of 33 lines × 127 mm. The other is a corrected proof of a description of Speculum Humanae Salvationis (1473), with a text of 35 lines × 132 mm. A number of manuscript notes by Morris for the catalogue – which he began on 21 May 1894 (Cockerell diary) – are extant, most plentifully in the Morgan Library. Morris planned to charge about five guineas for the catalogue (Cockerell to Macmillan & Bowes, 24 December 1895 [LB]). After his death the book had to be abandoned (but not before £17o Is. 4d. had been expended on it [Ledger]), though for a time there was talk of printing it elsewhere (Cockerell to Robert Proctor, 9 November 1896 (BL Add. Ms 52743, f. 238]). Instead Cockerell decided to combine the illustrations of woodcuts that had already been prepared (all but one by Walker & Boutall) with portions of an article by Morris, On the Artistic Qualities of the Woodcut Books of Ulm and Augsburg in the Fifteenth Century', Bibliographica, 1 (1895), 437-55. The book sold briskly before publication. ‘There has been a great rush on the German woodcuts & they were all sold out last week,' Cockerell wrote to Leighton on 3o November 1897. . . . A good many people have been left out in the cold, as orders keep coming in! I suppose it is the announcement that the Press is closing that has made people so eager'(JC). In another letter to Leighton, 14 December 1897, Cockerell said, At the last moment I have discovered a tiresome misprint in the colophon of ‘Some German woodcuts'- which necessitates a cancel. This will be finished tomorrow & can be fetched on Friday'(JC). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Inc. proofs (JC). (b) Proofs of process block illustrations (cuL Syn.1.89.97). (c) Proofs of process block illustrations (BL C.1o2.l.22). (d) Proof of front cover, initialled by W. H. Bowden (Jc). (e) Cockerell's copy, with marginal notes in Cockerell's hand, 8 labels bearing titles of early printed books in William Morris's hand’, and ‘a slip of paper addressed to Sir Sydney at the Kelmscott Press by Morris’(Cockerell sale, no. 54; Colin Franklin cat. 5 [Autumn, 1972], no. 96G). (f) Burne-Jones's copy (yc). (g) Pres. copy from Cockerell to William Lethaby, Christmas, 1897 (PML). (h) Pres. copy from Cockerell to Ernest W. Gimson, Christmas, 1897 (Aberdeen University Library). (i) Philip Webb's copy (τC). (j) Jenny Morris's copy (Fm[1]). (k) Pres. copy from Cockerell to Robert Proctor, Christmas, 1897 (Claremont). (l1) T. J. Cobden-Sanderson's copy (BC). (m) Copy (vellum) with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (Perry Collection). ## A5o William Morris, The Story of Sigurd the Volsung THE STORY OF SIGURD THE VOLSUNG AND THE / FALL OF THE NIBLUNGS [ornament 1] BY WILLIAM MOR-RIS [Troy type] COLOPHON: Here ends The Story of Sigurd the Volsung and the Fall of the Niblungs, written / by William Morris. With two pictures designed by Edward Burne-Jones and / engraved by W. H. Hooper. It was printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, / Hammersmith, and finished on the 19th day of January, 1898. / [paragraph] Sold by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press. CLLA1ON: Small 20: a⁴ b−08 [p]2. 10 leaves, pp. [8] [1] 2−207 [208– 10] [2] [=212]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Apple paper. Leaf: 328 × 231 mm. Text: 48 lines × 169 mm. TYPE: Text in Chaucer type; titles of books in Troy type. ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATIONS: Borders 33a and 33; side and corner borders; three-, four-, six-, and ten-line initials; section titles and shoulder titles in red; wood-engraved frontispiece and illustration on [p]r (p. [2o9]) designed by Burne-Jones. CoNTENTs: arr-ar blank a2r title a2ν blank a3r-a3 table of contents a4 blank a4 frontispiece bır-o8r text [p]ır illustration [p]1 colophon [p]r-[p]2ν blank. BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: 'SIGURD' [enlarged Golden type]. PUBLCATIN: 160 paper copies (6 gns.); 6 vellum copies (2o gns.) Issued 25 February 1898. NOTEs: Sigurd was first published in 1877. The earliest references to the KP edition are, as usual, in Cockerell's diary. 5 November 1891: Saw . . . a trial page of Sigurd. . . .'2o October 1892: Sigurd to be illustrated (perhaps) by B.[urne-]J. [ones] but cannot be thought of till the Chaucer [A40] is finished.'21 October 1895: w.M. full of the proposed new folio Sigurd with B-J illustrations.' However, only with great reluctance did Burne-Jones agree to illustrate the book. To pacify and please Morris I will do what I can to adorn the Volsung Tale,' he wrote to Lady Leighton in late 1895 (Memorials, ii, 276). ‘I shan't like doing it so much as Chaucer,’Burne-Jones commented to his studio assistant on 7 January 1896.‘The scenery for one thing isn't quite a painter's scenery – it's more a poet's’ (Rooke diary). On 5 May 1896 he complained, ‘It's quite wonderful how distasteful the task of making drawings of it is to me. . . . Terrible the tragedy of it is, and the fine things in it are so much fitter for literature than for my art, and I've no turn for the dramatic'(Rooke diary). As a result Burne-Jones made slow progress on the illustrations (which were to number about twenty-five), and in any case Morris's death meant the abandonment of the plans for a fully illustrated folio. Thirty-two copies of two folio pages (see Dı.9) were privately distributed to friends of Morris in January 1897. On the evening of 26 January 1897 Cockerell persuaded Burne-Jones to supply two illustrations for a more modest edition (Cockerell diary). Catterson-Smith redrew the designs (Cockerell diary, 6 October 1897). The two borders used in this book were almost the last that Mr. Morris designed. They were intended for an edition of The Hill of Venus, which was to have been written in prose by him and illustrated by Sir E. Burne-Jones. The foliage was suggested by the ornament in two Psalters of the last half of the thirteenth century in the library at Kelmscott House. The initial A at the beginning of the 3rd book was designed in March, 1893, for the Froissart [A4o], and does not appear elsewhere'(‘List’). CoNTENTS: [a]1−[a]3ν blank [a]4 title [a]4ν blank br-2k6 text 2k6ν colophon. BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). WRITTEN / BY WIL-LIAM MORRIS' [Chaucer type]. PUBLICATIοN: 300 paper copies (2 gns.); 10 vellum copies (10 gns.). Issued 25 February 1898. NOTEs: ‘This was the last romance by William Morris. He began to write it on Dec. 21, 1895, and dictated the final words [to Cockerell] on Sept. 8, 1896’ (‘List’). A trial page was printed on 19 May 1897 (Cockerell diary). In a letter to N. L. McMinn, 13 February 1928 (κm), Emery Walker explained how his firm, Walker & Boutali, produced the map on the front pastedown of The Sundering Flood and similar maps in the volumes of the Saga Library: either Morris or Magnússon, he said, would submit a rough sketch that included the names of places. An employee of Walker & Boutall – in this case H. Cribb – then drew a finished design, and a line block was made from it. The original plan was to place the map in the book proper, as Cockerell reported to Jenny Morris, 28 February 1898: .". . one or two find fault with the map being pasted inside the cover of the Sundering Flood. I knew this would provoke complaints, but it was done after much consideration, as the map was found not to harmonise in style, scale, or colour with the first page of the book, opposite to which it had been intended to put it'(BL Add. Ms. 52739, f. 52). Sheet b was reprinted because a border used on it was found to be unsatisfactory (see c below). For a discusssion of the emendations made by May Morris in the KP text, see Introductions, ii, 621-2. A less expensive edition of The Sundering Flood was published by Longmans in February 1898. (j) Another copy belonging to Cockerell, with a few Ms additions in his hand and designs and early proofs for the Golden type, proof of aborted design based on the Sweynheim-Pannartz type, Experimental capitals – printed in blue, the red added by hand by William Morris'(Cockerell's note), the first trial pages of The Glittering Plain (A1), design for border in King Florus (Azı), designs for initials, and letter from Burne-Jones about his frontispiece (Cockerell sale, no. 59; now PML). (k) W. H. Hooper's copy, interleaved with various designs and proofs (Claremont). (w) Copy (vellum) with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: ‘Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (Perry collection). ![](images/f45c43a0af711351b49e3dbf2b30608962887a385765e1bef7ba6c160e5e6468.jpg) 21 Initial word ‘THou', designed by Morris, which appears on p. 79 of The Works of Geoffrey Chaucer (A40). UNFINISHED BOOKS This category includes books that were planned and announced but eventually aborted and, in other cases, books that were mentioned by Morris but never really begun. The books that were more seriously contemplated by Morris are placed first in the list. ## B1 Froissart's Chronicles See NOTEs for A46 and D1.8. ## B2 Shakespeare's Plays On the flyleaf of a copy of The Heimskringla (1893) is the following note in the hand of Frederick J. Furnivall, founder of the New Shakspere Society: F. J. Furnivall, from Wm. Morris, at his Kelmscott House, Upr. Mall, Hammersmith, on Sunday evg., 26 Feb. 1893, when he assented (provisionally) to F.J.F.'s proposal that he should print a grand Shakspere in 3 vols. folio, edited by P. A. Daniel, or F.J.F., after his Chaucer (ed. F. S. Ellis) was done'(jDc). On 23 April 1893 Cockerell recorded in his diary, wM is to print a big Shakespeare for Longman.'However, in a letter to Frank E. Bliss, 23 July 1894, Cockerell observed that ‘the Shakespeare will not be ready for some time'(LB). In the British Library (C.1o2.1.22) there is a trial page on Perch paper of two columns of Golden type; shoulder titles, speakers' names, and stage directions are in red. Each column is 61 lines × 1o4 mm. The passage printed is Macbeth, Act 1, Scene viii. (Two copies of a slightly different trial page are known to be extant: one is in the Berger collection and one appeared in the Saks sale, no. 146. A trial page similar to the one in the Berger collection is reproduced as the frontispiece in Books and Letters Collected by William Harris Arnold of New York [Jamaica, N.Y., 1901].) By 1894 it was decided to issue the Shakespeare in eight or ten 80 volumes at about 25/- or 3o/- per volume. On 5 February 1895 Cockerell wrote to F. E. Thompson: ‘The Shakespeare is not yet in the press, as the Chaucer is absorbing all Mr. Morris’attention. It (the Šh.) will be a size between the Beowulf & the 8vo books unless present plans are altered'(LB). It is not surprising that Morris never found time to complete the Shakespeare. He once admitted to Cockerell, ‘Shakespeare did not much attract me, as I have not much sympathy with the dramatic form' (Introductions, ii, 677). ## B3 Vitas Patrum F. S. Ellis, the editor of this projected volume, wrote in an announcement (Cı5) issued in about February 1894: ‘This work, written by St. Jerome in the fourth century, was translated into English by William Caxton during the last years of his life, and is stated by his servant and successor to have been finished on the day of his death. It was printed at Wynkyn de Worde's press in 1495. . . . ‘Wynken de Worde's edition, which has never been reprinted, has become so rare that a fine perfect copy of the book has been sold for as much as two hundred and fifty pounds, and can rarely be met with even at that price. It is a book of very great interest alike to the philologist, the hagiologist, and the student of folk-lore. ‘It is proposed to proceed with the printing of a new edition uniform with that of Caxton's Golden Legend, issued from the Kelmscott Press in 1892, as soon as the name of one hundred and fifty subscribers have been received. In no case will more than two hundred copies be printed. It will make two large 4to volumes, and the price will be five guineas.' Cockerell wrote to Charles F. Carter on 7 August 1895: ‘Mr. Morris has abandoned the idea of printing “Vitas Patrum", as the number of subscriptions did not justify his going on with the book'(LB). For a specimen page, see DI.5. ## B4 Theodore Watts, The Coming of Love In a preface to the first and second editions of The Coming of Love (1898), Theodore Watts (afterwards Watts-Dunton) wrote: ‘Had it not been for the intervention of matters of a peculiarly absorbing kind – matters which caused me to delay the task of collecting these verses – I should have been the most favoured man who ever brought out\_a volume of poems, for they would have been printed by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press.' Parts of The Coming of Love had appeared in the Athenaeum and other periodicals, but some of the poems had been lent and lost; hence another reason for the delay in publication. The KP edition of 2oo copies was originally planned for publication in 1894 at two guineas, to appear simultaneously with John Lane's pp. [8] [1] 2-169 [170] [2] [=172]. I [1I] [ı1I] [IV] [V] [VI] [VI] [VI1I]’ [Golden tγpe on vol. i; reduced Golden type on vols. ii-vii]. PUBLICATION: 225 paper copies (3os. per volume); 6 vellum copies (7 gns. per volume). Vol. i issued 24 July 1896; vol. ii issued 17 September 1896; vol. iii issued 5 December 1896; vol. iv issued 22 January 1897; vol. v issued 9 March 1897; vol. vi issued 1 May 1897; vol. vii issued 29 July 1897; vol. viii issued 27 September 1897. NOTEs: Morris designed the first border in June 1895. I have been working hard at a border all day; but it has not come out right,’ he wrote to Jenny on 18 June. . . . never mind about the border. You see I am trying something new & can't expect to succeed at first go off’ (BL Add. Ms. 45340, f. 176). 'None of the ten borders used in The Earthly Paradise appear in any other book. The four different half-borders round the poems to the months are also not used elsewhere'(List’). The Earthly Paradise was first published in 1868-7o; Morris during the 186os laid plans, never realised, for an edition to be elaborately illustrated by Burne-Jones (see Joseph R. Dunlap, The Book That Never Was [New York, 1971] and A. R. Dufty's introduction to Morris's The Story of Cupid and Psyche [1974]). A single-volume, revised edition was issued by Reeves & Turner in 189o. For a discussion of the alterations in the KP edition, see Introductions, i, 105. This was the first kP book in which the Apple paper was used. ‘The size,' according to the list of ı June 1896 (C44), will be a new one, intermediate between the large 4to and the 8vo books hitherto issued.' To an interviewer in 1895 Morris commented: I am rather exercised as to its format but Cockerell is in favour of this new size – a sort of mild quarto, and yet looking like an octavo' (Ideal Book, p. 112). Cost of production was £855 18s. 7d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Preliminary drawings by Burne-Jones, possibly for llustrations (v & A[2] PD 2b). Only three volumes of the Earthly Paradise were finished in his lifetime and after his death the sheets were similarly submitted to me. sCc'(Cockerell sale, no. 47; Blackwell cat. 655, no. 382). The border round the poem for March was drawn by him but he was not satisfied with it & it was not engraved' (Cockerell's note) (Cockerell sale, no. 72; now Yale Ip. M834 868Dma).
