(γ) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 24 August 1896 (Sotheby cat., 22 December 1952, no. 78). ## A42 Laudes Beatæ Mariæ Virginis ## LAUDES BEATAE MARIAE VIRGINIS. COLoPHoN: These poems are taken from a Psalter written by an / English scribe, most likely in one of the Midland coun-/ties, early in the 13th century. / [paragraph] Printed by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press, / Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of Middle-/sex, and finished on the 7th day of July, 1896. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press. INSERTED NOTE (145 × 210 mm, Chaucer type; ‘Specimens', no. 128: 3oo. Perch'; distributed to subscribers after the book's publication and in most copies of the book): LAUDES BEATAE MARIAE VIRGINIS. / The Reverend E. S. Dewick has pointed out that these poems / were printed in 1579, in a 16mo volume, with the title Psalterium / Divæ Virginis Mariæ, rhythmice conscriptum a Reverendissimo / domino Stephano, olim Archiepiscopo Cantuariensi, ex Biblio-/theca Tegrinseensi depromptum, Cantandum sub melodia, Pa-/tris sapientia. Cum gratia & privilegio Cæsareo. Excusum Te-/gernsee. Anno MDLXXIX. This Tegernsee edition contains a / Conclusio of four verses in the same metre as the Aves, but the / text is otherwise inferior to that printed by William Morris. The / ascription of the authorship to Stephen Langton is doubly in-/teresting, as the manuscript transcribed for the Kelmscott Press / was probably written before his death in 1288. / Dec. 28th, 1896. COLLATION: Large 4°: [a]4 b-c8 d4. 24 leaves, pp. [8] [1] 2-34 [6] [=40]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Perch paper. Leaf: 290 × 210 mm. Text: 30 lines (6 poems of 5 lines each, with half-line spaces between poems) × 139 mm. TYPE: Troy type. ORNAMENTs: Half borders; six- and eight-line initials; texts of Psalms in red; alternate initials in blue. CoNTENTs: [a]1-[a]3 blank [a]4 title [a]4 blank br-dr text dIν colophon d₂r-d4 blank. BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). Text of title-page printed in black on front cover. PUBLICATION: 25o paper copies (Ios.); 10 vellum copies (2 gns.). Issued 7 August 1896. NOTEs: Morris purchased an early thirteenth-century English psalter, which he called the Nottingham Psalter, on 25 May 1893 for £115 (WMA B, p. 3s). One section of the Psalter, a Laudes beatae Mariae vir-. ginis, which Morris printed, was attributed either to Stephen Langton or to John Peckham, Archbishop of Canterbury. Other manuscripts of the Laudes exist. In the Nottinghan Psalter version, the opening words of each psalm are in red, followed by a quatrain which applies them to the Virgin. (For a description of the Psalter, which is now in the Morgan Library, see the Morgan Catalogue, iv, 37–40.) This was the first kP book printed in three colours. The trial pages (see b, c, and d below) reveal more than usual variation: two, for example, are set in the Golden and Chaucer types, and several are in black and red only. Cost of production was £86 11s. I1d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) ‘Trials of blue ink for B.V.M.'(Cockerell's note) (HEH Morrisiana 12’). (b) Trial pages (BL C.43.f.24 [∆b23] and [∆b12]). (c) Trial pages (HPL). (d) Trial pages (HEH Morrisiana 12’). (e) Cockerell's copy, with letter from the Rev. E. S. Dewick (see inserted note quoted above), twelve ‘coloured designs for initials', and two‘experimental woodcut initials in coloured inks'(Cockerell sale, no. 46). (f) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 21 August 1896 (NYPL). (g) Pres. copy from Cockerell to A. C. Swinburne, 8 January 1897 (Parke-Bernet cat., I1 January 1944, no. 542). (h) Philip Webb's copy (τC). (i) Jenny Morris's copy (Fm[1]). (j) Pres. copy from Cockerell: ‘To J.D. [Jane Duncan?] from the transcriber, Jan. 1oth, 1897'(Dc). (k) Copy (vellum) with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: ‘Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (pCL). (l) Pres. copy from Burne-Jones to J. W. Mackail, 26 August 1896 (wC). ## A43 Sir Thomas Clanvowe, The Floure and the Leafe and The Boke of Cupide THE FLOURE AND THE LEAFE, & / THE BOKE OF CU-PIDE, GÓD OF / LOVE, OR THE CUCKOW AND THE / NIGHTINGALE CoLopHoN: [ornament 1] Edited by F. S. Ellis, and printed by William Morris / at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in / the County of Middlesex, and finished on the 2ist day / of August, 1896. / {printer's mark 1] / [ornament 2] Sold by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press. NOTE ACCOMPANYING COLOPHON: Note [ornament 1] It has been pointed out by the Rev. Professor / Skeat, in “The Academy," May 2, 1896, p. 365, that the / words “Explicit Clanvowe" which occur in the MS., / Ff. 1, 6 (Camb. Univ. Lib.), clearly refer to the author of / the poem, Sir Thomas Clanvowe. He has further shown / that the date is about 1405-1o, and that “the Queen" is / Joan of Navarre, who held Woodstock manor. COLLATION: Medium 4º: [a]⁴ b–d⁸. 28 leaves, pP. [8], 1-47 [48]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Apple paper. Leaf: 234 × 163 mm. Text: 23 lines (including blank lines) × 118 mm. TYPE: Troy type. ORNAMENTs: Initial words WHAN (br) and THE (c8r); titles of poems and explicit in red. coNTENTs: [a]Ir-[a]3 blank [a]4 title [a]4 blank b1r-c7r text of The Floure and the Leafe c7 blank c8r-d7v text of The Boke of Cupide d8r note and colophon d8v blank. BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). Text of title-page printed in black on front cover. PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (1os.); 10 vellum copies (2 gns.). Issued 2 November 1896. NOTEs: Both poems were at one time attributed to Chaucer. Morris did not originally intend to include The Cuckow and the Nightingale; in a letter to Ellis, 8 April 1896, he wrote, You see I don't want to print anything but the Flour & the Leaf; I don't see any necessity for making it a thick book; & I think it would spoil the poem to couple it up with another. However let us wait & see how theF & L pans out when it is printed'(FM[1]: Ellis autograph album, f. 30). The two initial words were used earlier in the Chaucer (A4o). The first bound copy of the book arrived at Kelmscott House an hour or two before Morris's death (Cockerell to Robert Proctor, 9 November 1896 [BL Add. Ms. 52743, f. 238]). Wilfrid Scawen Blunt also mentions this circumstance, adding that Morris had never read The Cuckow and the Nightingale and was waiting to do so for it to be in print' (Blunt diary, 6 June 1898). Cost of production was £31 16s. 1od. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Trial pages (BL C.43.C.26 [ΣFF20] and C.- 43.f.24 [∆bı2]). (c) Copy with note by Cockerell: ‘This first specimen of this book, with the side unlettered,,arrived at Kelmscott House a few hours before William Morris died, on October 3, 1896'(Cockerell sale, no. 49; Quaritch cat. 773 [4 December 1957], no. 363). (d) Burne-Jones's copy (Sotheby cat., 7 July 1919, no. 226). (e) Pres. copy from Jane Morris to Philip Webb, 6 November 1896 (τC). (f) Jenny Morris's copy (FM[1]). (g) Annie Cobden-Sanderson's copy (Blackwell cat. 487, no. 285). (h) Pres. copy from Cockerell to Mr. and Mrs. T. M. Rooke, Christmas, 1896 (wc). (l) Pres. copy from Ellis to Miss Coleridge, 24 August 1897 (American Art Association cat., 1 March 1933, no. 436). ## A44 Edmund Spenser, The Shepheardes Calender [two ornaments 2] THE SHEPHEARDES CALENDER: / CON-TEYNING TWELVE AEGLOGUES, / PROPORTIONABLE TO THE TWELVE / MONETHES. COLLATION: Medium 4°: a⁴ b-g8 h⁴. 56 leaves, Pp. [8] 1-98 [6] [=104]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Apple paper. Leaf: 234 × I63 mm. Text: 32 lines × 101 mm. TYPE: Golden type. PUBLICATION: 225 paper copies (1 gn.); 6 vellum copies (3 gns.). Issued 26 November 1896. NotEs: The volume was edited by Ellis, but by an oversight the names of author, editor, and artist were omitted from the colophon' (‘List’). The illustrations were printed from process blocks made by Emery Walker's firm, Walker & Boutall. Cost of production was $\mathcal { E } 7 5$ 6s. 1od. (Ledger). NOTEs: Morris ‘began [his] new romance in verse’on 4 February 1895, but on 8 February after seeing B[urne]-J[ones] w.M. decided to write the new romance in prose & verse, & began to rewrite it' (Cockerell diary). Eventually Morris wrote it in prose alone. (The verse fragment is in BL Add. MSs. 45322-3, 45325.) ‘It was first announced as in the press in the list of June 1, 1896 [see C44], at which date the early chapters were in type, although they were not printed until about a month later' (‘List’). In Cockerell's diary we catch glimpses of the dying Morris attempting in vain to complete the design of the initial word ‘Empty’. 4 September 1896: ‘He sat up & was going to design “Empty"for the 3rd part of The Water of the Wondrous Isles – but only just managed to black in the spaces, & just rough in the word.'6 September 1896: ‘w.M. rather tired, but decidedly no worse. He had done a little more to “Empty".' But, as the colophon indicates, Catterson-Smith had to finish the design. May Morris believed that her father had seen only the first two parts of the book through proofs (Introductions, ii, 575), though Cockerell was convinced that Morris had read more proofs than that (Cockerell to May Morris, 14 September 1912 [BL Add. Ms. 5274o, f. 12]). May helped in proofing the rest of the book (Cockerell to May Morris, 19 January 1913 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 14]). Cost of production was £158 1os. 3d. (Ledger). (m) Copy (vellum) with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (pCL). ## A46 Froissart's Chronicles (specimen pages) No title-page. CoLopHoN: [ornament 1] These two trial pages of the projected / edition of Lord Berners'translation of / Froissart were printed at the Kelmscott / Press in September, 1897, to preserve the / designs made for the work by William Morris [ornament 1] In the border are the arms of / France, the Empire, and England; on the / second page are those of Reginald Lord / Cobham, Sir Walter Manny, and Sir John / Chandos. COLLATION: 20: [a]². 2 leaves, PP. [4]. VELLUM & DIMENSIONs: Vellum. Leaf: 424×283 mm. Text: two columns of 63 lines × 87 mm ([a]1v) or 94 mm ([a]2r) each. TYPE: Text in Chaucer type; title of Prologue in Troy type. ORNAMENTs: Border 32 (which includes a twenty-one-line initial word); half border; three- and ten-line initials; chapter titles and shoulder notes in red. CONTENTs: [a]I colophon [a]1ν-[a]2r text [a]2ν blank. BINDING: Not bound. PUBLICATION: No paper copies; 16o vellum copies (1 gn.). Issued 7 October 1897. NOTEs: In 1895 Morris described his projected Froissart in these words to an interviewer: You cannot have a better text than old Berners's. It's fine old English and would take a lot of beating. The edition of the text I am using is the two volumes quarto published by Rivington and others in 1812. My reprint is a full folio and will take up two volumes. I also intend to publish it in four parts. Those who may like a strong binding will be able to get one from me for both the Chaucer [A4o] as well as the Froissart. It will be in white pigskin – a beautiful material for wearing and showing designs – and the designs will be by Cobden-Sanderson. . . . no book that I could do would give me half the pleasure I am getting from the Froissart. I am simply revelling in it. It's such a noble and glorious work, and every page as it leaves the press delights me more than I can say. I am taking great pains with it, and doing all I can to realise what I have long wished (Ideal Book, Pp. 1II-12). Berners’ translation of Froissart (Morris's copy is now at kM) had been a favourite of Morris's since his college days. As soon as his Troy type was delivered from the foundry, he had' a single-column trial page set up in January 1892 (see b below); early in 1893 double-column trial pages in the Chaucer type were produced (List’). In March of that year Morris was showing initials designed for the Froissart to his friends (Cockerell diary, I March 1893). On 8 October 1893 Cockerell noted in his diary: ‘E[dward] B[urne]-J[ones] had that morning offered to do a big St. George & some shields of arms for the Froissart, which will put the book on the shelf for a while. Jason to be printed instead.' The subject of the frontispiece of volume i was to be St. George, that of volume ii Fame; it was understood that Emery Walker would do the heraldic research (Introductions, ii, 43s). Sparling was to be the editor (Sparling, p. 111). Though it was several times announced as in the press, the Froissart continued to be held in abeyance while work on the Chaucer progressed. It was definitely abandoned only after Morris's death. In November 1896‘about thirty-four pages were in type, but no sheet had been printed' (‘List’). During the following month thirty-two copies of sixteen of those pages (see Dr.8) were privately distributed to friends of Morris. ‘This suggested the idea of printing two pages for wider distribution. The half-border had been engraved in April, 1894, by W. Spielmeyer, but the large border only existed as a drawing. It was engraved with great skill and spirit by C. E. Keates, and the two pages were printed by Stephen Mowlem, with the help of an apprentice, in a manner worthy of the designs'(List’). According to a note by Cockerell, only about a half-dozen (perhaps not so many) copies'were printed with the colophon on the first rather than the last page, ‘as I came to the conclusion it was a mistake' (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], no. 202). This statement seems to be one of Cockerell's rare inaccuracies, because on all the copies I have examined the colophon appears on the first page. A facsimile of these specimen pages was produced by Frederick Goudy, the American type-designer, in 1918. When Swinburne received his copy of the sixteen pages, he wrote to Cockerell, 2o January 1897, ‘A thousand thanks for your beautiful & precious gift. Why did not Morris take up & finish Froissart earlier – instead of the less appropriate work (Shelley, for one instance) on which the Kelmscott Press was occasionally wasted'(PML MA 3000 R-V). Cost of production, including all expenses incurred for the abandoned Froissart, was £1o8 5s. 7d. (Ledger). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Designs for eight initials (CL ‘red portfolio'). (b) Trial pages, some with preliminary drawings of coats of arms, and inc. proofs (Bc). (c) Trial pages, designs for ornaments and initials, and a ‘list in Morris's autograph of the Coats-of-arms that would be needed, with notes by Sir Emery Walker' (Cockerell sale, no. 76). (d) Inc. proofs (su). (e) Inc. corrected proofs (Saks sale, no. 174). (f) Philip Webb's copy (πc). (g) Copy with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: ‘Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897’ (Perry collection). ## A47 Sire Degrevaunt ## SIRE DEGREVAUNT CoLopHoN: [ornament 2] Edited by F. S. NOTEs: The text is based upon the Camden Society's Thornton Romances (see NOTEs for A33), in which James Halliwell wrote that ‘Sir Degrevant . . . is, perhaps, more curious and valuable than the [other romances in the volume]. The descriptive notices of early costume and architecture, are of peculiar interest; and it would perhaps be difficult to select a romance of the kind of more merit'(p. xxii). The story had long been a favourite in Morris's circle: in the summer of 186o Burne-Jones did a wall-painting of The Wedding Procession of Sire Degrevaunt in the drawing room of Red House, Morris's home. The text follows closely the Halliwell version except that brackets around conjectural readings are omitted. A more recent editor of the romance has described the KP edition as ‘a pleasant book to read, but useless for textual study' (L. F. Casson, ed., The Romance of Sir Degrevant [Early English Text Society, 1949], p. xv). The frontispiece was not printed until October, 1897, eighteen months after the text was finished' (List’). On 18 October 1897 Cockerell wrote to Leighton, Please send for the sheets of Syr Degrevaunt & the blue papers for the covers, on Friday. You will then be able to go ahead & to issue Syr Isambras [A48] & Syr Degrevaunt together at the end of the month'(LB). A flimsy was inserted by Leighton between the frontispiece and the first page of the text, the only instance in which this was done in any κP book. Cost of production was £48 3s. 7d. (Ledger). Cockerell (ed.), Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century ## SOME GERMAN WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH / CENTURY COLOPHON: [[e]6r] Here ends Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century, for / which the blocks (with one exception) were prepared by Walker / and Boutall under the direction of the late William Morris. Now / edited by S. C. Cockerell, and printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper / Mall, Hammersmith. Finished on the 1sth day of December, 1897. / [[e]6v] [printer's mark 2] / Sold by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press. FOREwORD: Of the 35 reproductions of German woodcuts here given, 29 are all / that were done of a series chosen by William Morris to appear in / the Catalogue of his library, which was to have been annotated by / him and printed at the Kelmscott Press. The other 6 were made for / his article in the 4th number of Bibliographica on the Early Wood-/cut Books of Ulm & Augsburg, and thanks are due to Messrs. Ke-/gan Paul, Trench, Trübner & Co. for the loan of the blocks, as well / as for permission to quote the portions of the article which serve as / an introduction to this book. These quotations will show the nature / of the remarks that Mr. Morris intended to write for the Catalogue, / of which they would have been the most interesting feature. A minor / feature was to have been a statement of the number of different cuts, / and the number of cuts including repetitions, in the principal illus-/trated books. For this purely scientific side of the work I was made / responsible, and as the early printers usually repeated their cuts, and / no one has hitherto troubled to count them in this way, it has seemed / worth while to preserve these few statistics now that the original de-/sign of the Catalogue has fallen through. They are accordingly print-/ed as an appendix in a list of the more important woodcut books that / the library contained. For some particulars in this section I am / indebted to my friend Mr. Robert Proctor, who has kindly looked / over the proofsheets, and pointed out errors and omissions. / [paragraph] I have been tempted to add a list of the manuscripts, which, though / comparatively few in number (112) were the most notable part of the / library; but without a full description of each volume such a list would / be meaningless, and anything more than a list would be out of place. / S. C. CÔCKERELL. CoNTENTs: π title πı blank ar foreword (by Cockerell) ai blank a2r-a4 introduction (by Morris) a4v-π2r A LIST OF THE WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH / CENTURY REPRO-DUCED IN THIS BOOK.'π2 blank [b]r-[d]7 illustrations [d]- 7v blank [d]8r-[e]5 [ornament 3] A LIST OF THE PRINCIPAL BOOKS OF THE / FIFTEENTH CENTURY, CONTAIN-ING WOOD-/CUTS, IN THE LIBRARY OF THE LATE WILLIAM / MORRIS. Printed in black on front cover: ‘SOME GERMAN WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH CENTURY' [Golden type]. PUBLICATION: 225 paper copies (3os.); 8 vellum copies (5 gns.). Issued 6 January 1898. NOTEs: Morris asked Cockerell ‘to help him to catalogue his library' on the evening of 19 October 1892 (Cockerell diary), and for the next few years Cockerell, in addition to his other duties, studied and catalogued Morris's extensive collection of incunabula and mediaeval manuscripts. Out of this activity grew Morris's scheme of a heavily illustrated catalogue of his library, with descriptions by Cockerell and additional notes by himself. To an interviewer in 1895, Morris said, ‘This will be largely illustrated with facsimiles of the plates and pages of the book themselves. Of course, this is just a little fancy for my own pleasure, and yet I have an idea that there ought to be many book-lovers and art-lovers who would find much in the volume to delight them. . . . You see that I am sparing no trouble to give the finest reproductions’ (Ideal Book, p. 112). Two trial pages of the projected catalogue have survived (BL C.43.- f.24 [∆b12] and C.102.h.18). The first is a description of a psalter, N.E. France, c. 126o, on a leaf of Flower paper (281 × 204 mm), with a text of 33 lines × 127 mm. The other is a corrected proof of a description of Speculum Humanae Salvationis (1473), with a text of 35 lines × 132 mm. A number of manuscript notes by Morris for the catalogue – which he began on 21 May 1894 (Cockerell diary) – are extant, most plentifully in the Morgan Library. Morris planned to charge about five guineas for the catalogue (Cockerell to Macmillan & Bowes, 24 December 1895 [LB]). After his death the book had to be abandoned (but not before £ı7o Is. 4d. had been expended on it [Ledger]), though for a time there was talk of printing it elsewhere (Cockerell to Robert Proctor, 9 November 1896 (BL Add. Ms 52743, f. 238]). Instead Cockerell decided to combine the illustrations of woodcuts that had already been prepared (all but one by Walker & Boutall) with portions of an article by Morris, On the Artistic Qualities of the Woodcut Books of Ulm and Augsburg in the Fifteenth Century', Bibliographica, 1 (1895), 437-55. The book sold briskly before publication. ‘There has been a great rush on the German woodcuts & they were all sold out last week,' Cockerell wrote to Leighton on 3o November 1897. . . . A good many people have been left out in the cold, as orders keep coming in! I suppose it is the announcement that the Press is closing that has made people so eager'(JC). In another letter to Leighton, 14 December 1897, Cockerell said, At the last moment I have discovered a tiresome misprint in the colophon of ‘Some German woodcuts’- which necessitates a cancel. This will be finished tomorrow & can be fetched on Friday' (JC). RELATED MATE RIALS: (a) Inc. proofs (JC). (b) Proofs of process block illustrations (cuL Syn.1.89.97). (c) Proofs of process block illustrations (BL C.1o2.1.22). (d) Proof of front cover, initialled by W. H. Bowden (Jc). (e) Cockerell's copy, with marginal notes in Cockerell's hand, ‘8 labels bearing titles of early printed books in William Morris's hand’, and ‘a slip of paper addressed to Sir Sydney at the Kelmscott Press by Morris'(Cockerell sale, no. 54; Colin Franklin cat. 5 [Autumn, 1972], no. 96G). It was printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, / Hammersmith, and finished on the 19th day of January, 1898. / [paragraph] Sold by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press. COLLATION: Small 20: a⁴ b−08 [p]². 110 leaves, PP. [8] [1] 2-207 [208– 10] [2] [=212]. PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Apple paper. Leaf: 328 × 23I mm. Text: 48 lines × 169 mm. TYPE: Text in Chaucer type; titles of books in Troy type. ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATIONS: Borders 33a and 33; side and corner borders; three-, four-, six-, and ten-line initials; section titles and shoulder titles in red; wood-engraved frontispiece and illustration on [p]ɪ (p. [2o9]) designed by Burne-Jones. CONTENTS: aI-aI blank a2 title a2 blank a3-a3 table of contents a4 blank a4 frontispiece bır-o8 text [p]ı illustration [p]1 colophon [p]1-[p]2ν blank. NOTEs: Sigurd was first published in 1877. The earliest references to the KP edition are, as usual, in Cockerell's diary. 5 November 1891: ‘Saw . . . a trial page of Sigurd. . . .’ 2o October 1892: ‘Sigurd to be illustrated (perhaps) by B.[urne-]J.[ones] but cannot be thought of till the Chaucer [A40] is finished.'21 October 1895: ‘w.M. full of the proposed new folio Sigurd with B-J illustrations.' However, only with great reluctance did Burne-Jones agree to illustrate the book. ‘To pacify and please Morris I will do what I can to adorn the Volsung Tale,' he wrote to Lady Leighton in late 1895 (Memorials, ii, 276). ‘I shan't like doing it so much as Chaucer,’Burne-Jones commented to his studio assistant on 7 January 1896. ‘The scenery for one thing isn't quite a painter's scenery – it's more a poet's’ (Rooke diary). On 5 May 1896 he complained, It's quite wonderful how distasteful the task of making drawings of it is to me. . .. Terrible the tragedy of it is, and the fine things in it are so much fitter for literature than for my art, and I've no turn for the dramatic'(Rooke diary). As a result Burne-Jones made slow progress on the illustrations (which were to number about twenty-five), and in any case Morris's death meant the abandonment of the plans for a fully illustrated folio. Thirty-two copies of two folio pages (see Dr.9) were privately distributed to friends of Morris in January 1897. On the evening of 26 January 1897 Cockerell persuaded Burne-Jones to supply two illustrations for a more modest edition (Cockerell diary). Catterson-Smith redrew the designs (Cockerell diary, 6 October 1897). The two borders used in this book were almost the last that Mr. Morris designed. They were intended for an edition of The Hill of Venus, which was to have been written in prose by him and illustrated by Sir E. Burne-Jones. The foliage was suggested by the ornament in two Psalters of the last half of the thirteenth century in the library at Kelmscott House. The initial A at the beginning of the 3rd book was designed in March, 1893, for the Froissart [A4o], and does not appear elsewhere'(‘List’). RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Intermediate drawing for second illustration (wmG). (b) Four preliminary drawings by Burne-Jones probably intended for Sigurd (v & A[2] 1.11o). The lower three were never engraved, as Morris's death on 3 Oct 1896 put an end to the project' (Cockerell's note) (BL C.102.1.22). (d) Trial pages (BL C.102.1.22). Finished on the 1sth / day of November, 1897. / [printer's mark 1] / Sold by the Trustees of the late William / Morris at the Kelmscott Press. COLLATION: 80: [a]4 b-2i8 2k6. 258 leaves, PP. He began to write it on Dec. 21, 1895, and dictated the final words [to Cockerell] on Sept. 8, 1896'(List’). A trial page was printed on 19 May 1897 (Cockerell diary). In a letter to N. L. McMinn, 13 February 1928 (κM), Emery Walker explained how his firm, Walker & Boutali, produced the map on the front pastedown of The Sundering Flood and similar maps in the volumes of the Saga Library: either Morris or Magnússon, he said, would submit a rough sketch that included the names of places. An employee of Walker & Boutall – in this case H. Cribb – then drew a finished design, and a line block was made from it. The original plan was to place the map in the book proper, as Cockerell reported to Jenny Morris, 28 February 1898: . . . one or two find fault with the map being pasted inside the cover of the Sundering Flood. I knew this would provoke complaints, but it was done after much consideration, as the map was found not to harmonise in style, scale, or colour with the first page of the book, opposite to which it had been intended to put it'(BL Add. MS. 52739, f. 52). Sheet b was reprinted because a border used on it was found to be unsatisfactory (see c below). For a discusssion of the emendations made by May Morris in the KP text, see Introductions, ii, 621-2. A less expensive edition of The Sundering Flood was published by Longmans in February 1898. UNFINISHED BOOKS This category includes books that were planned and announced but eventually aborted and, in other cases, books that were mentioned by Morris but never really begun. The books that were more seriously contemplated by Morris are placed first in the list. ## B1 Froissart's Chronicles See NOTEs for A46 and D1.8. ## B2 Shakespeare's Plays On the fyleaf of a copy of The Heimskringla (1893) is the following note in the hand of Frederick J. Furnivall, founder of the New Shakspere Society: ‘F. J. Furnivall, from Wm. Morris, at his Kelmscott House, Upr. Mall, Hammersmith, on Sunday evg., 26 Feb. 1893, when he assented (provisionally) to F.J.F.'s proposal that he should print a grand Shakspere in 3 vols. folio, edited by P. A. Daniel, or F.J.F., after his Chaucer (ed. F. S. Ellis) was done'(jDc). On 23 April 1893 Cockerell recorded in his diary, wm is to print a big Shakespeare for Longman.'However, in a letter to Frank E. Bliss, 23 July 1894, Cockerell observed that‘the Shakespeare will not be ready for some time' (LB). In the British Library (C.1o2.1.22) there is a trial page on Perch paper of two columns of Golden type; shoulder titles, speakers' names, and stage directions are in red. Each column is 61 lines × 1o4 mm. The passage printed is Macbeth, Act 1, Scene viii. (Two copies of a slightly different trial page are known to be extant: one is in the Berger collection and one appeared in the Saks sale, no. 146. A trial page similar to the one in the Berger collection is reproduced as the frontispiece in Books and Letters Collected by William Harris Arnold of New York [Jamaica, N.Y., 1901].) By 1894 it was decided to issue the Shakespeare in eight or ten 8º volumes at about 25/- or 3o/- per volume. On 5 February 1895 Cockerell wrote to F. E. Thompson: ‘The Shakespeare is not yet in the press, as the Chaucer is absorbing all Mr. Morris’attention. It (the Sh.) will be a size between the Beowulf & the 8vo books unless present plans are altered’ (LB). It is not surprising that Morris never found time to complete the Shakespeare. He once admitted to Cockerell, Shakespeare did not much attract me, as I have not much sympathy with the dramatic form' (Introductions, ii, 677). ## B3 Vitas Patrum F. S. Ellis, the editor of this projected volume, wrote in an announcement (Cıs) issued in about February 1894: ‘This work, written by St. Jerome in the fourth century, was translated into English by William Caxton during the last years of his life, and is stated by his servant and successor to have been finished on the day of his death. ‘It was printed at Wynkyn de Worde's press in 1495. . . . ‘Wynken de Worde's edition, which has never been reprinted, has become so rare that a fine perfect copy of the book has been sold for as much as two hundred and fifty pounds, and can rarely be met with even at that price. It is a book of very great interest alike to the philologist, the hagiologist, and the student of folk-lore. ‘It is proposed to proceed with the printing of a new edition uniform with that of Caxton's Golden Legend, issued from the Kelmscott Press in 1892, as soon as the name of one hundred and fifty subscribers have been received. In no case will more than two hundred copies be printed. It will make two large 4to volumes, and the price will be five guineas.' Cockerell wrote to Charles F. Carter on 7 August 1895: ‘Mr. Morris has abandoned the idea of printing “Vitas Patrum", as the number of subscriptions did not justify his going on with the book' (LB). For a specimen page, see D1.5. ## B4 Theodore Watts, The Coming of Love In a preface to the first and second editions of The Coming of Love (1898), Theodore Watts (afterwards Watts-Dunton) wrote: ‘Had it not been for the intervention of matters of a peculiarly absorbing kind – matters which caused me to delay the task of collecting these verses - I should have been the most favoured man who ever brought out a volume of poems, for they would have been printed by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press.' Parts of The Coming of Love had appeared in the Athenaeum and other periodicals, but some of the poems had been lent and lost; hence another reason for the delay in publication. The kP edition of 2oo copies was originally planned for publication in 1894 at two guineas, to appear simultaneously with John Lane's cheaper edition (Thomas Hake and Arthur Compton-Rickett, The Life and Letters of Theodore Watts-Dunton [1916], i, 276). Even after Morris's death Cockerell called on Watts and Swinburne to discuss the book on 19 October 1896 (Cockerell diary), and the following day Cockerell drafted a memorandum on the subject (see E8). ## B5 A Catalogue of William Morris's Library See NOTES for A49. ## B6 Cupid and Psyche According to Georgiana Burne-Jones, One of many plans now ended [with the death of Morris in 1896] had been to publish an illustrated book of The Hill of Venus. It was to have been rewritten and have had ornamental borders made for it by Morris; the pictures, redrawn, were those designed by Edward long ago for The Earthly Paradise. The question as to whether the story should be written in verse or prose was settled by Morris saying, “Well, you know, old chap, prose looks blacker in the page and fills up better – so it's to be prose" (Memorials, ii, 290). However, after calling on Burne-Jones on 8 December 1896, Cockerell recorded in his diary: EB-J talked about continuing the Kelmscott Press – & wanted his Cupid and Psyche drawings to be recut & printed.'The plan was that Catterson-Smith would, as usual, redraw the designs on photographic copies, and Hooper would cut the new blocks; the wood-engravings would be accompanied by the text of the Cupid and Psyche’section of The Earthly Paradise, set in Troy type. Seven specimen quarto pages were printed at the kp in January 1897, and it was arranged, according to a note by Cockerell, ‘that the book should be printed after the close of the Kelmscott Press'. The text area was to be 159 × 105 mm (HRC: Emery Walker papers). A memorandum between Morris's trustees, Burne-Jones, and Longmans, Green (who were to publish the volume) is dated February 1897 (wMG K.774). The project was finally abandoned after Burne-Jones's death in 1898. ## B7 The Bible In a letter to Blunt, 4 December 189o, discussing her husband's plans for printing, Jane Morris wrote, a nice bible is much needed, but the Universities only have the right to print that'(FM[1]). ‘He [Morris] has thought of printing . . . the Bible,' Cockerell noted in his diary on 1o September 1891. Mackail, in a letter to Cockerell, 6 March 1899, recalled that Morris first thought of reprinting the Authorised Version but later favoured Wycliffe's translation (BL Add MS. 52734, f. 23). Cockerell wrote to James G. Clarke on 3 October 1894, Mr. Morris has sometimes spoken of printing the Bible, but it would probably be in folio, & in any case it is too big an undertaking to be thought of until the Chaucer & Shakespeare are finished'(LB). Several extant Burne-Jones drawings, illustrating episodes in the Bible and the Apocrypha, may have been intended for this project (WMA B, p. 11; Kenneth A. Lohf, The Burne-Jonesiness of Burnejones’, Gazette of the Grolier Club, Ns No. 17 [May 1972], Pp. 46–70). ## B8 Gesta Romanorum ‘The black letter fount is almost ready, and he [Morris] thinks of making a beginning with John Ball. Chaucer to follow – also perhaps “Gesta Romanorum"’(Cockerell diary, 22 October 1891). Morris owned several editions of the work (Library, nos. 415-16, 888-9). ## B9 Some English Mediaeval Songs and Music So listed by Cockerell (List’). The book of mediaeval English poetry I remember myself discussing with him [Morris] (or rather, taking part in a discussion between him & Burne-Jones) as early as 1892. But it was one of the things that he repeatedly took up & laid aside again (Mackail to Cockerell, 6 March 1899 [BL Add. Ms. 52734, f. 23]). ## B1o John Bunyan, The Pilgrim's Progress w.M. talked of printing The Pilgrim's Progress (at Bernard Shaw's suggestion)'(Cockerell diary, 1o December 1894). ## Bı A Book of Ballads Mr. F. S. Ellis at K.[elmscott] H.[ouse.] Talk about a Book of Ballads to be printed at the Kelmscott Press'(Cockerell diary, 26 January 1894). ‘He [Morris] was engaged on the selection of the Ballads, which he spoke of as the finest poems in our language, during his last illness' (‘List’). ## B12 The Douce Apocalypse On 3 September (misdated August) 1894 Emery Walker wrote, on behalf of Morris, to E. W. Nicholson, Librarian of the Bodleian Library, requesting permission to photograph and publish in facsimile a thirteenth-century apocalypse (Ms. Douce 18o) known as the Douce Apocalypse (Bod.: Applications to Facsimile, 1889-94). On 28 November 1894 Walker and Morris spent the day at the Bodleian examining the manuscript, which had been a favourite with Morris when he was an undergraduate (Bod., L.P. entry book; note by Walker on letter from Morris to Walker, [3o October 1894] [íRC]). ## B13 The Musical Compositions of Henry vIII “Amongst other editions which he (Morris) hoped to have printed," says “The Saturday Review,"“was a volume of musical compositions by King Henry vı1. and the English composers contemporary with him. The project originated with Sir Edward Burne-Jones, who was to have designed the woodcuts; and the music, which was to have consisted of the contents of two Mss. in the British Museum, Add. Mss. 31,922 and 5,665, was to have been edited by Mr. Arnold Dolmetsch. The project was carried so far that the photographing of the Ms. was actually commenced"'(Aymer Vallance, William Morris [1897], p. 414). ## B14 Edward FitzGerald, The Rubáiyát of Omar Khayyám Cockerell's diary reveals that he suggested this title to Morris on at least three occasions. 11 June 1891: Suggested that Morris should print decent editions of Omar Khayyam and of“On the Nature of Gothic" from the Stones of Venice. Morris seemed to think that he would do so, as The Golden Legend is such a big book that he will have to do some little ones after it is finished.’21 April 1892: “Suggested that WM should print a collection of his best loved poems including Omar Khayyam.’1 November 1892: I begged him to printOmar Khayyam, and I think he will do it. In 1872 Morris, with the assistance of Burne-Jones and Fairfax Murray, had produced an illuminated Ms of The Rubáiyát (now in BL Add. MS. 37832). ## B15 Scott's Novels He [Morris] has thought of printing Scott's novels as well as Chaucer and the Bible, after he has completed printing the Golden Legend and all his own books!!'(Cockerell diary, 1o September 1891). ## B16 Dickens'Novels In a letter to Frances Horner, probably written in November 1893, Burne-Jones said, . . . Morris vows he will print Dickens (if he lives) at the Kelmscott Press' (FMʃ1]). Morris hinted at something like this to an interviewer in 1895 (Ideal Book, p. 107). ## B17 John Ruskin, Unto This Last Morris declared his willingness to print “Unto This Last" at the Kelmscott Press . . .'(Cockerell diary, 24 March 1892). However, in a letter to Morris of 2o January 1893, George Allen, Ruskin's publisher, rejected as too expensive Morris's terms of £i per copy for an edition of 300 copies (HEH HM 36676). ## B18 William Morris, The House of the Wolfings C. M. Gere, another nice young Birmingham artist, called in the aftn. with some drawings, and w.M. asked him to have a shot at illustrating the Wolfings’(Cockerell diary, 12 December 1892). During 1893 and 1894 Gere produced a series of drawings, which Morris criticised in some detail. (His letters to Gere are in the Huntington Library.) Trial pages in Chaucer and Troy types are extant (BC; BL C.102.h.18; BL C.43.f.24 [∆b12]), as is a design for a border (BL C.- 43.c.26 [ΣQQ46]). Gere's drawings, annotated by Morris, are in a private collection in England. ## B19 William Morris, The Roots of the Mountains Morris intended to have A. J. Gaskin illustrate it (Cockerell diary, 23 April 1893). A trial page in Chaucer type was struck off (BL C.102.h.18; BL C.43.f.24 [∆b12]). ## B2o Poems by Ralph Waldo Emerson Late in 1894 F. Holland Day, of the Boston publishing firm of Copeland & Day, approached Morris about printing a selection of Emerson's poems, which would be co-published by Copeland & Day in America. In a letter to Day of ı January 1895, Cockerell outlined Morris's terms: 16/- per paper copy and £3 1os. per vellum copy for an edition of 3oo or 35o (LB). But in the meantime Morris had agreed to print Hand and Soul (A36) for another American publisher; hence on 27 June 1895 Cockerell wrote to Day again, saying, I fear that there is now very little chance of the Emerson being begun this year, other things having been taken in hand'(LB). ## B21 Others For the following titles I have found no documentation other than citation by Cockerell in his ‘List’. 1. The High History of the San Graal, translated by Sebastian Evans. 2. Piers Ploughman. 3. Huon of Bordeaux. 4. Caxton's Jason. 5. A Latin Psalter. 6. The Prymer or Lay Folk's Prayer-Book. 7. Malory's Morte Darthur. ADVERTISING CIRCULARS During the first year of its existence the κP did not advertise its books, probably because most of them were fully subscribed by the date of publication. As Cockerell explained later, ‘We only send out special announcements of books which are not nearly sold out when they go to the binder' (Cockerell to W. Glaisher, 24 October 1895 [LB]). The circulars proved to be effective: Cockerell noted in his diary on 1 October 1894, for example: Lots of k.P. orders owing to circular sent out on Friday.'The first of the lists to include titles ‘in preparation’, that of 1 December 1892 (C3), was prompted by an inaccurāte report in a London newspaper, and it ‘was necessary', said Morris, ‘to clear up the matter by a rational statement of what books were in preparation' (Morris to Quaritch, 1 December 1892 [BQ]). In the following entries the circulars are printed on leaves of the Flower paper of approximately 21o × 143 mm unless otherwise indicated. The Whatman paper used for a few of the earliest circulars was stock left over from the large-paper edition of The Roots of the Mountains (189o). Title numbers not accompanied by a letter are titles listed in Section A; italic numbers indicate titles announced for the first time. Unless otherwise noted, the circulars are printed in black ink only; ‘two colours' means both black and red ink are used. The Morris & Co. lists were distributed directly by Morris & Co. to their customers rather than by the KP. ## Cr List, May 1892 (‘Specimens', no. 2.) Two leaves (191 × 161 mm), Whatman paper. Two colours. Golden type. Proof: su. Corrected (by Morris) proof and revise: BL C.102.h.18. ALREADY PRINTED: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6. IN THE PRESS: 7, 12, 8. No vellum copies are mentioned. In the proof the following titles are also listed as in preparation: 40, 50, 19, 12. ## C2 List, July 1892 (‘Specimens', no. 3.) Two leaves (195 × 16o mm), Whatman paper. Two colours. Golden type. ALREADY PRINTED: I, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6. IN THE PRESS: 7, I2, 8,9, 10. The ‘Specimens' copy may be a proof, as it is on different paper. ## C3 List, December 1892 (‘Specimens', no. 4.) Two leaves (196 × 163 mm), Whatman paper. Two colours. Golden, Troy, and Chaucer types. Corrected proof (copy of July 1892 list [C2], revised by Morris and Sparling): BL C.- 102.h.18. Annotated (by Sparling?) copy: PML ‘Morrisiana II'. AL-READY PRINTED: I, 2, 3, 4, S, 6, 7, 8, 9, 12, 1O. IN THE PRESS: 13, 15, 11, 39. IN PREPARATION: 22, 14,40. Again, the Specimens'copy is on different paper. Printer's mark I and the specimens of the three type faces (‘This is the Golden type. / This is the Troy type./ This is the Chaucer type.’) appear for the first time. There were two issues of this list: in the second, the number of copies of The Well at the World's End (A39) was increased from 3oo to 35o, the publisher of Shakespeare's Poems (Aı) – Reeves and Turner – was added, and misspellings of C. M. Gere's name and of L'Ordene were corrected. ## C4 List, 9 March 1893 (‘Specimens’, no. 1.) Four leaves (194 × 162 mm), Whatman paper (but also some on Flower paper). Two colours.
